Chapter 1

Chapter 1: Fuck Magic

Cracked linoleum slits my knees like spite. The kind of petty cut you don't notice until it's stinging through your trousers and the adrenaline backs off. My breath fogs against the kind of winter air that doesn't just invade but squats in corners like it's paying rent. This cheap community "alley"-clinic stinks of half-sterilized bandages, mana-rot, burnt hair, and the quiet panic that lingers when death overstays its welcome. Faintly, the sound of royalty-free "muzak" haunts its way through the air, the Infomercial on the waiting room TV turned up. The girl beneath me convulses again.

"Shh… shh, hey. You’re okay. You’re not—"

Her spine jerks, back arching like a snapped bowstring, mouth frothing in crescents of pink. Her ribs rattle like a bag of old dice under my hands. Blood where there shouldn't be blood, breath where there shouldn't be gaps. Every twitch is a clock tick closer to an obituary. She's losing air, time, and odds—all at once. And me? I'm gambling with Magic.

She’s maybe fifteen. Just a kid. Poor thing couldn't even get herself through the door. Pulse in freefall. Every rune-monitor in this slapdash trauma bay screams in a different dialect. A salvaged electrocardiogram kit whines next to a busted cauldron half-full of thaumic coolant. Someone has duct-taped a sanctified camphor talisman to the ceiling. Another has scrawled "LOVE IS A MIRACLE" over it in marker. Yeah? Try billing that to the regulator council.

I don't have time to guess how long she's been dying, or how poorly I set this place up, or why the universe keeps leaving corpses at my feet and asking for a receipt. She's here. I'm here. No one else is.

I press my palms to her sternum. Cold skin. Too light. The bones feel like they belong to someone who never finished growing.

"ThRun-kel Tuu Vaar'lenna"
No Echo. No Decay.

A pulse kicks from deep in my diaphragm as a Null Field snaps into existence. The air distorts, rippling outward like the heat shimmer on asphalt, then stills. Reality—mundane, lawful, sanitized physics—gets politely evicted. The field blooms. Spherical. Fragile. A dome of anti-reality laced with stolen spells and prayers I barely believe in. The "Old Speech" tends to have that kind of environmental impact, but I'm going for broke here. The standard Med Protocol Statis Field would not be able to handle the severity of her wounds, or the consequences if what I'm about to do is detected.

The tattoos etched across my forearms—entropy wards carved in ink and nerve endings—ignite. Pale violet. Huh. That's new. Questions for later.

"Shul'dara Vek Thae-lun"
Burn the end into Birth

I start the incantation. The real one. No Academy-template safezone spells. No healing-lite first-aid authorized by thaumaturgical ethics boards. This is real magic, raw and unlicensed, hates being bent into shape.

Each word tears its way up my throat like barbed wire. The null field hums louder, resisting the feed. Magic floods my limbs, burning as it threads itself through my arms. Through scar tissue from doing this sort of thing once-too-often, and spent muscle from just having a day-and-a-half. I force it down, into my wrists, into my fingers.

Her wound slowly start shuddering as they stitch themselves together, previously-spilled ichor tracing its way back inside it's host.

Not fast enough. Not nearly fast enough

She's drowning inside herself. Organ failure. Lungs filling. My field tenses. Feedback spirals along my spine. The limiter—every damn student has one, coded into our IDs, our aura signatures, even the cuffs of our coats—snaps back like a whip.

I push harder, audibly snarling. Focus. I redirect flow from my shoulders into the small fractures in my hands. The scars glow brighter –faint, ethereal cracks leaking sickly magenta light. My nails crack from the pressure.

"Come on," I mutter. "Come on, breathe."

Another pulse. Runes etched into the null field flare too wide, too sharp. If anyone nearby has a regulator beacon tuned, they’ll feel that. Screw it. I keep going.

"Vaelun"
"Please."

Not a spell. A plea.

Her alveoli begin to reknit. Capillaries reroute. Blood reverses direction like I'm unwinding her death.

Then—a single inhale. Wet, but real.

She jerks. Once. Then goes limp.

Still.

Still.

And then—

A breath.

Holy shit.

She's alive.

For a single heartbeat, the world holds.

And then the null field screams.

Every rune-lamp in the alley explodes in sequence. Light. Shatter. Feedback. The regulator beacon embedded in the mana flow pings out like a flare. I’d bet half my tuition credits someone three blocks away just felt that. The spell collapses inward, folding the null field like paper into itself. Entropy backlash punches through my arms, singeing nerve endings raw. The air smells of ozone, blood, and divine sarcasm.

Sirens bloom on the horizon. Not the friendly kind.

I’m running out of time.

"Nihil sanctus. Resurgo."

The final phrase stings my gums as I seal the spell. I play it safe, ending the Triplet with Latin for stability, even if leaning on mass-production spellwork tastes a bit wrong in my mouth. Internal lattice reweaves. Circulatory rhythm stabilizes. Her color improves.

I wrap her in a silver-foil thermal, tucking the edges under her elbows. I reach into my coat, unhook the entropy-seal clasped to my wrist—my tracker, my confession, my crime—and place it beside her fingers.

Footsteps thud. Not fast. Intentional. Crimson-armored Regulators appear at the alley mouth in a staggered diamond formation. Wands already drawn. Suppression wards vibrating across their forearms.

Eight of them. Full complement. They were expecting worse.

"Hands where we can see them!"

I stay kneeling.

I raise my hands.

Magic still dances in my bones. My grip on it loosens as I let it go.

"I was just practicing my thesis," I say.

Helmet visors don’t crack smiles. Worth a shot.

"Step away from the girl."
"She’s stable. If you touch her before the field fully fades, she’ll spike and you’ll have killed her."

They don’t care. Two step forward.

"Uh-uh, fellas. Florence Nightingale's got that sweet chin music for you if you touch my patient".

Knowing their attention needs to be kept on me, I lean back on my heels and sit myself on my rear, my legs splayed and oh-so-conveniently blocking their path to the teen. The two give a glance at one another, before rejoining the group to form a half-circle. One begins the arrest ritual, voice flat, like I’m just another datapoint.

"Name. Student ID. Magical license class."

I tilt my head. Real slow. Let the silence drag an extra beat. Then I twist my lips into the most exaggerated, cartoonishly unimpressed pout I can muster—upper lip out, nose crinkled, eyelids half-mast like I’ve just smelled bureaucratic rot baking in the sun.

"No."

I really let that syllable drag out. Not shouted. Not whispered. Just... stated. Like gravity. Or spite. I swear even their hoods looked shocked.

"You refuse to comply?"
"I'm declining to comply, Chief"
I say, the vowels dripping with the sacred indignation of a Looney Tune on his last carrot.
"Subtle difference."

Somewhere in the back of my head, I hear a faint wahh-wahhhh fade in the distance. One of the Hoods with a Golden North Atlantic Magic Federation Insignia emblazoned on his right-arm gauntlet steps forward.

I wonder if he can see my smirk behind that visor.

A wand muzzle touches my temple. It hums.
Yeah. He saw it.

"Unauthorized miracle-working in a restricted zone."
"Emergency clause."
"No academy supervisor signed off."

That’s it. That’s the moment.

I throw my arms up in full-body disbelief, like I’m trying to scoop the sheer stupidity out of the air with my hands.

"She was dying! That's what makes it an emergency, you phenomenal DUNCE!"

My hands are flying. I’m gesturing like a man explaining fire to cavemen. One hand clutches at my own hair while the other points furiously at the void where this specimen’s common sense should be. I'm stunned—stunned—that this conversation is real.

Then—

"And you are not cleared to make that call!"

He says it like it’s checkmate.

I let out a laugh that’s more exhale than joy. Stare at him like I just caught a goose trying to recite algebra.

"Oh, give your balls a tug!"

I sneer, but internally I feel a bit of my fight deflate, my hands dropping to their side with it. Of course not. I'm not a licensed miracle-worker. Just a student. Just a cleric-in-training with a chip on his shoulder and a naive deathgrip on his remaining conscience.

They slap the cuffs on. Cold metal laced with dampening sigils bites into the skin above my entropy scars. Numbness radiates. A sob tries to rise in my throat, but I’ve already learned how to kill those before they show.

How do lawyers not break down in "Frustration TearsTM" when they're arguing in court?? Goddamn Sociopaths.

I look down one last time. She’s breathing. God's above, she's actually still breathing. Sounds like a wet maraca, but it's there, and steady. Good. At least, way better than it was ten minutes ago.

I exhale. The cuffs hiss.

"Fuck magic."

The shackles click shut.

[AUDIO FRAGMENT: FILE_CORRUPT_04 // SOURCE: VIRAL_FEED]

Let's talk about magic.

"The supernatural and mysterious power of apparently influencing a course of events." The ultimate cop-out for writers the world over. "Spooky action at a distance" if you're Einstein-inclined.

Great. Those are the definitions you’d get if you asked a bored teenager who just binge-watched the entire Harry Potter franchise in one sitting. Magic, as mundanes tend to understand it, is basically a shortcut to doing stuff that would normally require effort, intelligence, or batteries. Which is hilarious, considering how much work it actually takes.

I refuse to bother with a "real definition" for concept so nebulous. It's a wonder anyone can even pretend to define it. You already know what Magic is. Or at least, you think you do. Fireballs. Lightning. Glowy runes. Whatever.
If it's shiny and not a mushroom, it's probably magic. How's that for a rigorous academic approach?

But what's really going on behind the curtain of sparkles and special effects? What is Magic?

I'll tell you.
Magic is Some Ol' Bullshit.

By and large, I'd say Harry Potter is to blame for people's preconceptions. Harry asks Hagrid why the magical world is secret and gets some trite spiel about how "ALL the muggles" would want to start using magic to solve all of their problems.

When the HELL has anything worked that way?

Everything has a cost. Especially magic. And that cost is rarely financial. Though in modern society, magic tends to follow the same principle everything else does: "If you're good at it, never do it for free." If you want to get something, you have to give something up. And with Magic, you never quite get or give what you expected to.

You know, while I'm on the topic, let's address the whole "muggle" and "mudblood" thing. NO ONE is investing time, effort, and energy on special slurs to call people based on magical lineage.
Well…some people might but there are weirdos like that everywhere, we don't consider outliers. Trust me, we already have more than enough slurs…

Case in point! My family is from India; I was raised as a citizen of the illustrious North Atlantic Magical Federation (a.k.a Woodbridge, New Jersey).

The Federation has had access to the internet and cell phones as long as it's Mundane Counterpart; someone called me the "n-word" in traffic on Thursday. The Magical and Mundane peoples are just as advanced (and just as backwards) as one another. Humans are the same no matter where you go.

We call that "progress".

The wagon stinks of ozone, piss, and bureaucratic boredom. Chain-runes hiss every time I shift, crackling in some ancient dialect designed to shame sorcerers before laws even existed. They line the bars like barbed wire made of light, looping lazy helixes across the metal mesh of the cage. Cold bleeds through the floor, right into my spine. Every time the wagon hits a bump, the chain-loop under my seat flares a glyph in warning—reminding me that one twitch too far will sear through the skin.

I sit cuffed, knees jammed against the slatted walls, ankles throbbing from where a dampening glyph kissed bone. The whole transport feels like an apology for compassion: cramped, loud, and rolling at a crawl down back alleys too narrow for emergency service but somehow wide enough to haul away unlicensed medics. The roof leaks condensation from spell-dampened air, and every drop that lands on me feels like the system spitting.

Two Regulators sit opposite me, crimson armor gleaming like sin in the pulse-light. Helmets full-face, visors opaque. They haven’t spoken since they shoved me in the cage. Probably trained not to. If they said anything, it might sound like doubt.

"You know,"
I say, loud enough to make their posture twitch,
"in some countries, healing without a license is called 'heroism.'"

No reply. Fine. I wasn’t really talking to them anyway.

"Bet the girl makes it,"
I add, tone too casual to be anything but pointed.
"Wouldn’t that be awkward? Alive and breathing and…not regulated, none of it."

The one on the left tilts their helmet a hair. A shift smaller than a nod, but I clock it. Progress. Or maybe checking their rune-feed for violations of the local noise ordnance.

"Just think,"
I continue, leaning back until the chain-runes tug sharp at my wrists,
"if I'd let her die, none of this paperwork would be necessary.
No reports.
No tribunal.
No sleepless nights wondering about all the regulations I broke
and if they're thinking about you too."

Still nothing. No verbal response, no twitch, no weight shift. But I catch the subtle tightening of a gauntlet, the telltale flutter of overclocked heart rate in the ambient hum of the suppression ward. The one on the right's pulse just spiked. Bingo.

"All those Combat Magic courses
they have you guys running must be
winding you up too tight,"
I say, furrowing my brow in a performance of concern.
"Must be hard, carrying all that armor and none of the moral weight. Heeeavy stuff, boys, heavy stuff."

That gets a reaction. Barely. The right one's shoulders twitch like he wants to shrug off the insult and can't decide if that counts as breaking formation.

"They must teach you all sorts of high-octane badassery, right? What is it these days? Arcane CQC? Wand-kata? Tactical Pilates? Wait wait, no I got it! Hot yoga!"

I lean forward with a grin I know will get under his skin.

"C'mon, big guy, what’s the secret sauce?
Do you guys chant in unison,
paint slogans on your chests before beating up street kids?
Or is it more of a spontaneous thing?"

The tension in the hothead's jaw is now a tectonic event. He hasn't moved, but the restraint in his posture is screaming. The kind of scream that comes right before a punch.

"Okay,"
I say, like I'm offering a lifeline.
"First answer that comes to mind:
One hundred of your elite unit versus one silverback gorilla—who wins?"

SLAM.

The sound is a thunderclap. The impact knocks the air out of me before I process the pain. My head cracks against the steel mesh wall with a dull thud. Stars burst behind my eyes. Tinnitus screams in both ears. Copper floods my mouth.

Hothead's up, gauntlet braced across my throat, the bulk of his armor pressing me into the van's reinforced wall like he's trying to meld me with it.

"KEEP. YOUR MOUTH. SHUT"

Each word is a hammer. Or maybe that was his fist. Each pause, a countdown. The kind of tone that tells you this isn't about me—not really. This is about something already cracked inside him and me showing him the fracture line.

My chains rattle as I raise my hands a few inches, chuckling painfully—not in surrender, just enough to show I'm not going to fight him. Not yet.

I lean my head back as far as the wall allows, staring up at the sigil-riddled ceiling.

"Not trying to provoke you,"
I croak, voice hoarse with pressure and pride.
"Well… not only. I'm just trying to fill the silence."

Before it fills me first, the last part I leave unsaid.

He holds there for a beat. Two. Breathing hard. The regulator's gauntlet trembles for half a second before he pushes off, steps back, and resumes his silent post like nothing happened. Like I imagined it. Like he's not one weak insult away from kicking my ribs in.

I cough, blood in my teeth now, and smile like I’m proud of it. Because I am.

Next checkpoint's close. I can feel it in the way the hum of the runes syncs with my heartbeat.

Good. I’m starting to run out of things to say.

The city outside blurs by. I try not to track it too closely. New Canaan's alleys all look the same when you're on your way to get publicly flogged by bureaucracy. But the moment we round a corner and the ward-glass clears, I get a clean look at the skyline. It hits like always: a punch to the throat made of glass and privilege.

Above the slums: the sky-bridges. Floating over half-frozen gutters and blown-out wards, they gleam like myth. Suspended by ley-tethers and taxpayer arrogance, the Academy towers connect overhead like the bones of some divine spinal cord. Clinics up there don’t use salvaged glyph-lamps or bootleg entropy seals. They have name-brand miracles. Branded. Billable. One floor for necrotics. Two for the elite-class trauma ward. Three for the insurance-backed rebirth suites.

A perfect place to get sick, if you can afford it.

Below, the street-level looks like a field hospital designed by a committee of vultures. Blood-slick slush slops under our wheels. Half the rune tags on the buildings are faded, overwritten with gang sigils or protest glyphs. I spot one that says "HEAL US OR HAUNT US" in spray-rune. Can't tell if it's a threat or a prayer. Someone tagged a ward-drain with a stencil of the Angel of Entropy, wings half-melted.

"Tell me,"
I ask the Regulators,
"when you signed up to protect the magical public interest,
was criminalizing CPR part of the recruitment poster?"

Stone silence. They’ve either muted their helm-audio or filed me under ‘rhetorical hazard.’

I shift again. Chain-runes bite my wrist like they resent my pulse. Probably do. These things were forged to bind deviance, not dialogue. Every move I make pings a silent report to the Academy’s Enforcement Matrix. I imagine some overworked functionary back at HQ scrolling through my vitals like a bored ex.

"Ever wonder what a miracle's worth?"
I murmur.
"Not in credits. In consequence."

Still nothing. Maybe they don't have clearance to talk about metaphysics. Maybe they just don't get paid enough.

We roll to a stop.

The gate checkpoint. Reinforced sigil-barrier, three layers deep, staffed by more crimson meat-suits holding arc-pikes like they were born gripping them. The wagon lurches as it docks into the intake platform. A dozen anti-teleport wards spark briefly as the runes verify we’re not smuggling eldritch contraband or curse-slugs. I sit very still, mostly to savor the irony. Once, this same checkpoint let me through with a lunchbox full of Class II healing runes and no questions asked.

The lead tech – some poor bastard who looks about fifteen under their helm – waves a wand through the window slit.

"Subject: Rao, Bodhi.

Suspected Class IV unauthorized miracle-working.

Confirmed thaumic residuals.

En route to Academy Custody."

One of the watchers glances at the readout. Her jaw tenses.

"Cleric track?"
"Fourth-Year,"
the other replies.
"Shit."

The sound is barely audible, but I catch it. She covers with protocol.

"Proceed."

The wagon rolls on.

Through the next viewport I see a boy – maybe twelve – watching from behind a chain-fence, bundled in a coat five sizes too big. Her Brother I realize in recognition. I barely clocked the kid before, too preoccupied with saving the girl 's- his sister I remind myself -life. There is the faint recollection of him screaming bloody murder as I shunted him inside the clinic and set up my makeshift Trauma Bay in the alley. When our eyes meet, he makes the sign of healing. Not the Academy one. The old one. Fingers curled, thumb to sternum. Passed down by grandmothers who never made it past orientation exams. The kind of sign you learn when your miracles are all secondhand.

I give him a wink and a quick smile; the boy was worried to bits about his older sister, no need to stress him out any further.

The cage jolts as we pass under another suppression arch. Somewhere behind me, an etheric scanner starts logging my aura decay. It’ll show entropy scars flaring on contact with the girl. It won’t log the hesitation in my hands. The silent prayer. The look in her brother's face when she finally stopped seizing. The breath that wasn’t supposed to come back.

None of that goes in the file.

"You know what your problem is?"
I say to the rune-bars.

They don’t answer.

"You're efficient.
You're clean.
You're quiet.
You erase stories as well as scars."

Still nothing.

But the runes glow a little dimmer. Or maybe that's just me.

Next stop: Containment Ward 7-C. Cold fire, automated scanners, and a medic who thinks I’m a contaminated tool.

Can’t wait.

Chapter 2

Chapter 2: The Scalpel & The Scribe

[AUDIO FRAGMENT: FILE_CORRUPT_04 // SOURCE: VIRAL_FEED]
...look, maybe I should back up. Definitions matter. The old guard wants you to think Magic is some mystical favor from the Gods. Screw that.
By-and-large, the magical world is wrapped up in the same veneer of Civility as the Mundane. And to the detriment of all involved, is entrenched in the same level of bureaucracy, hypocrisy, and corruption.
But I digress!
...where was I?
Right. "Magic."

1. "Magic Isn’t Scientific"

Wrong. Magic follows laws. Conservation, Entropy, Flux, Displacement. It has principles just like physics. It’s just... messier. Less Newton, more Schrödinger. Magic is a science, like anything else.

Understand this: everything that exists in our reality, exists within the confines of the Rules of Reality. Just because something does not fit into our understanding of the world, does not mean it violates it.

Some time in the 40s, a few magically inclined physicists came to a conclusion that would have turned the World on its head, if the information wasn't surpressed.

At its core, Magic is the fifth fundamental force of the universe. Right up there with gravity and whatever it is that keeps your atoms from vibing out of existence. Except this one has an on-again/off-again relationship with order and loves loopholes.

There’s a whole ass list of magical laws. Thermodynamic knockoffs. Newtonian reboots. Gauss with jazz hands. We call ‘em the 12 Laws of Thaumaturgy. They matter - but only if you plan on living through spellcasting.

Magic displaces reality. Literally. It creates a void where the laws of physics collapse and new rules can take over. Think of it as reality having a panic attack. Physics takes five, and Magic strolls in with a Venti double espresso and a grudge. If reality were a bathtub, Magic is your favorite "'bubber' ducky". It "displaces" the water around it. We call that duck the Thaumaturgic Field. It’s everywhere. It permeates every atom, molecule, and soul. It’s carried by particles we call Thaumions - basically quantum middle fingers to the Standard Model. And trust me, they’re real. We’ve measured them. Every spell ripples through the Thaumaturgic Field like a fart in a wind tunnel - invisible to those without the "gift", but undeniably real. Thank the thaumions for that.

Frosted steel bites. My wrists sing the bruise‑colored hymn they started rehearsing the moment the rune‑cuffs first locked, each note sharper than the last. A crimson‑armored Regulator nudges me forward with the flat of his warded baton. The corridor ahead is iced in thaumic blue, ceiling‑mounted auric strips thrumming like hive wings. The air tastes of antiseptic pine and latent ozone— the Academy’s signature perfume for don’t touch, don’t breathe, comply. I check the strip's pulse rate out of habit; 60Hz. Standard for containment, but the yellow undertone means the backup generators are cycling. Sloppy.

Beneath the hum of the pumps, a speaker system plays a distorted, time-stretched loop of "Toxic" by Britney Spears, retuned to 432Hz for "maximum patient pacification." It’s working; a violent calm settles over me.

I shuffle past a security panel, wincing as the decontamination mist hits the gash on my left eyebrow. Dried blood pulls tight—an ironic tilak, paired with the entropy‑scar constellations glowing a timid violet beneath my skin. Great. I probably look like a discounted saint in the clearance aisle.

"Step," the Regulator drones, voice modulated through a brass half‑mask etched with anti‑contagion sigils.

His partner raises a palm, rune‑plate flaring, and a transparent containment door iris‑spins open. Containment Ward 7‑C scrolls in holographic letters across the threshold. Underneath: BIO‑THAUM RISK: SEVERE.

Intensive care for inconvenient miracles— that’s what 7‑C is. Where the Academy parks anything or anyone too magically radioactive to mingle with the donor‑banquet crowd upstairs. Today I’m the bleeding‑edge exhibit. My eyes half-close. If they want to treat me like a specimen, I might as well act like one.

My boots squeak traitorously on the slippery disinfectant film. Above the ceiling tiles, mana-vacuum pumps whine at a pitch that rattles my teeth—a sub-audible drill into the skull. The air tastes of astringent ethanol layered over burnt rosemary, distinct as a chemical cocktail. Sterility flavored with failed ward incense. Everything is over-lit, over-white; even shadows look laundered here.

A pair of articulated arms descend from the ceiling rig the moment I cross the gasket line. They’re chromed, jointed like praying mantis legs, and end in crystal‑studded rings that dilate to encircle my chest. Needle-thin runes of spell-light probe through skin, bone, soul. Focus. Detox first, existential crisis later.

I keep my sarcasm on an external monologue setting— easier to file complaints with Reality when you narrate.

A phosphorescent diagnostic rune scrolls above me, projecting vitals I don’t need to see: ENTROPY LOAD: 173 μE. PHYS‑ENERGY DEFICIT: −2 800 kcal. SPIRITUAL NOISE: MODERATE. Basically I’m an over‑turned battery leaking acid on the carpet.

Automaton Medic #26 glides forward on rail‑wheels embedded in the floor. Humanoid chassis, alabaster fiber‑resin, face a blank porcelain plate with a single runic lens where a nose would be. An aura‑speaker crackles.

"Subject designator Bodhisunya Rao, unlicensed healer. Processing detox protocol D‑441. Hold still."

Hold still, he says, as if the restraining rings haven’t already turned my ribs into compression art. A spray of cold mist envelops my arms— neutralizer aerosol laced with auric chelators. Feels like being dipped in dry ice and shame.

"Friendly PSA," I grit out through clenched teeth. "The patient preference field on your bedside forms says ‘he.’ I’m a student, not a lab rat."
"Detox commencement: 15… 14…13…"

A violet surge ripples across my forearms, entropy wards flaring defensively. The automaton’s sensor registers the spike; mechanical arms ratchet tighter. Tendons creak. Pain booms behind my eyes.

The world tilts. For a split second, the pain tastes like mint and the sound of the machine's gears looks like jagged yellow lightning. Synesthesia override. The limiter's kicking in.

The protocol runs in three vicious acts:

  1. Thaum‑Ion Extraction: Needle‑thin conduits breach skin, siphoning rogue particles. Each extraction feels like a tiny sun being yanked through capillaries.
  2. Entropy Scrub: Nanoscopic glyphs crawl over my nerves, shaving runaway probabilities back into compliance; every shave is a razor dipped in nostalgia— I relive the alley clinic in flashes: cracked linoleum, girl’s ribs fluttering, sirens screeching.
  3. Limiter Sync: My student ID cuff— the same one that throttled me mid‑spell— is attuned to the mainframe here. It re‑imposes safe‑casting thresholds by burning a brand into the metaphysical equivalent of marrow. The process tastes like burnt cinnamon and regret.

By the time the countdown reaches zero I’m panting, shirt plastered to spine, eyes watering from ozone stench. The automaton retracts its claws.

"Detox complete. Probability deviation reduced to Academy standard. Subject remains bio‑thaum risk due to unstable entropy signatures."
"File it under feature, not a bug," I rasp. My voice rides a raspy, half‑drunk chuckle— more defiance than humor.

The rings detach and retreat into the ceiling. Gravity is obscene without their support; I nearly collapse before my knees catch. Two Regulators step inside, wands leveled—you could slice bread on their righteousness.

"Hands behind—" one starts.

I comply, palms outward, entropy scars still glowing faintly.

"Dude, relax. I’m environmentally friendly now— zero‑emission cleric. Just don’t shake me, you’ll void the warranty."

No laugh. Philistines. They usher me to a magnesium‑polished examination plinth. Every step squeaks. My soles leave watery boot‑prints that evaporate in seconds, devoured by ambient ward‑heat.

The medic returns with a tray of opalescent phials. Each is labeled THAUM‑CREDIT ASSESSMENT SAMPLE. A little meter hovers overhead, ticking like a parking clock. Even here— especially here—the ledger rules. The Academy can’t bill me for unauthorized miracle‑working until they quantify exactly how much miracle I leaked onto the alley floor.

The automaton unsheathes a humming lancet rune.

"Blood and aether sample required for tuition damage appraisal," it explains in a voice blended from two hundred bored bureaucrats. "Failure to comply will result in asset seizure."
"Take a number," I mutter, presenting my forearm. "I’m worth at least four kidneys on the thaum‑organ market. There’s a discount for bulk."

The lancet kisses skin— bright sting, then numb. The phial fills with shimmering, ink‑dark fluid threaded by violet sparks. My blood looks like a night sky in a test tube. Poetic or biohazard? Why not both.

The meter chirps and calculates. Projected fine: 89,000 lumens. Lumens— the Federation’s idea of magical fiat currency, pegged to the average mana cost of a Class II shield rune. Eighty‑nine grand for saving a kid. That's three years of Dad's ventilator bills. Or a decade of indentured servitude in the mana mines if I default. My stomach hollows.

"Itemized receipt?" I ask.
Sarcasm is my respirator; inhale at your own risk.

The automaton prints a vellum slip. Services Rendered: Emergency Null‑Field, Unlicensed Regeneration, Municipal Ward Damage (minor), Regulator Deployment (level B mobilization). Below the total: PAYMENT PLAN AVAILABLE— INTEREST ACCRUES DAILY.

I laugh— a cracking glass noise. "Any chance I can pay in existential dread? I’ve got surplus."

"Humor detected. Humor flagged as potential defiance response. Recommending disciplinary oversight."

"Put it on the stupid tax."

Adjacent countertops are littered with half‑dissected manikins, rune‑etched organ jars, and crimson binders titled Miracle Efficiency Index—Q4. They logged my transgression as a data set.

Here’s the Academy’s math: unauthorized healing equals uncontrolled variables. That terrifies grant committees. Better to let a girl bleed out than risk statistical anomalies in miracle yield.

The detox lamps are jealous little suns. They hang from the ceiling like metallic fruit, each one pulsing a sequenced ultraviolet strobe meant to burn residual magic out of flesh. Every third pulse slams into my retinas and ricochets into the back of my skull, leaving after‑images of jagged runes that refuse to blink away. I try to look anywhere else, but the room offers only polished steel, reinforced glass, and the reflection of a handcuffed idiot who tried to save a stranger.

The idiot looks tired. His cuffs— thaumically graded adamantite, because of course we can’t skimp on theatrics—are secured to a standing restraint column shaped like a six‑armed tuning fork. The bolt‑ring at my spine buzzes with low‑grade suppression wards, just strong enough to make every lymph node feel like it’s full of crushed ice. My tattoos—entropy wards carved into skin last semester during an ill‑advised practical on harmonic scarring—still glow under the ultraviolet, but the light robs them of color, bleaching violet into something sickly and wrong.

A wave of nausea climbs my spine— part detox, part fury. My fingers flex involuntarily, entropy crackling. I force a deep breath— count the sensory anchors:

  • Cooling coolant smell in distant vents.
  • Sub‑bass throb of mana turbines.
  • Sharp tang of copper on tongue.
  • Clinical white glare prickling eyes.
  • Heartbeat— heavy, syncopated.

I swallow anger. Not here, not yet.

A maintenance automaton trundles by outside the glass partition, spray‑nozzles hissing. It pauses, identifies me as "Biohazard Candidate," and mists the corridor with a lemon‑pine antiseptic that coats the back of my throat in sweet chemical dread. The smell mixes poorly with the copper ghost of my own burned mana still lodged behind my sinuses.

I keep checking the chrono‑glyph projected on the far wall. 22:15. 22:16. Each minute drags nails across the chalkboard of my patience. Where is he? The entire Academy knows I melted half a regulator grid re‑knitting a girl’s lungs. Public‑relations crisis, ethics violation, textbook recklessness—pick your scandal flavor. That alone should have Hartnell sprinting down the halls like his starched lab coat’s on fire.

Unless, of course, he’s rehearsing the narrative. Drafting the press release where he’s the diligent surgeon blindsided by a rogue student. Polishing the language so "Bodhi Rao" rhymes with "irresponsible martyr."

"Subject displays elevated cortisol, 38 percent above baseline. Recommending sedative rune application."
I raise a brow. "Bill me extra for the lullaby?"

22:19. Footsteps at last. Not the clipped, baton‑steady march of Regulators; these heels are softer, arrogant. The door rune‑scans an approaching badge, decides the newcomer outranks everyone within fifty meters, and swishes open with a hissed salute.

The machine lifts an injector sigil; I flinch, readying a counter‑glyph. Before escalation can bloom, another voice ripples down the corridor—smooth, cultured, heavier than the gravity in the room.

"Stand down, Medic #26. The council wants him alive and conscious for questioning."

Footsteps approach— leather soles, unhurried, expensive. I know that cadence: Dr. Lucien Hartnell, Staff Medical Surgeon, absentee guardian of the alley’s dying girl. Scene 4’s upcoming antagonist.

The moment crystallizes; I can practically feel the narrative baton passing, but Scene 3 isn’t done milking me yet.

Regulators snap to stiff attention. The automaton retracts the injector, placing it delicately back on the tray.

A red rune flashes on the wall monitor: CODE BLUE: WARD 4B.

Someone screams down the hall. A junior medic freezes, panic in his eyes.

Dr. Lucien Hartnell glides into view. He doesn't break stride. He doesn't even look at the monitor. He simply raises his left hand, snaps his fingers, and flicks a stabilization glyph—perfectly calculated to the patient’s entropy signature—through the wall. The alarm dies instantly. The screaming stops.

He saved a life like he was swatting a fly.

Then he turns that gaze on me. Immaculate white coat, high collar starched into an exclamation point, hair silvered at the temples. His surgical mask hangs loose around his neck. Diamond‑point cufflinks wink thaumic gold sigils: Primum non nocere—First, do no harm. The irony prickles.

Behind him float two assistant scribes—etheric drones shaped like elongated teardrops of blue light. One of them pulses red as I shift my weight, logging a potential aggression vector. They don't just record; they curate. Every twitch, every elevated heartbeat, every sarcastic aside is being hashed, indexed, and fed into a liability algorithm before I even speak.

Hartnell’s eyes rake the room, cataloguing equipment like a head chef spotting fingerprints on silverware. Then he fixes me with a look polished to a mirror sheen. I know that look. Professional disappointment, version 3.6. The one reserved for interns who drop priceless vials and for departmental budgets that arrive one digit short.

He pauses, eyes skimming readouts on my file projected in mid‑air. His jaw tightens. I see the moment he clocks the 89 000‑lumen liability tied to my name— a liability linked to his department’s budget because he was off playing god‑knows‑what while a kid flat‑lined.

For a second, the projection flickers, overlaid by a memory I can't blink away: a girl's face, grey as ash, blood blooming on pavement like a dark flower. I taste copper. The sarcasm catches in my throat. I didn't save her because of the budget. I saved her because she was scared and I was the only goddamned thing standing between her and the Void.

The door’s runic lock disengages with a polite chime.

I crack a grin, blood from the split lip finally thawing enough to trickle.

"Doctor Hartnell," I croon, voice low, a blade ribboned in velvet. "Glad you could join the fuck‑up you left on hold."

Shock rolls through the ward like silent thunder— the Regulators stiffen, the automaton recalculates threat matrices, the containment lamps flare a defensive yellow. Hartnell’s expression slides from controlled annoyance to ice‑sheen rage.

Good. Rage means leverage.

But that, as bards say, is another scene’s problem.

The containment lamps dim back to sterile white. The automaton withdraws, wheeling its tray of my priced‑out miracle to some accounts‑receivable abyss. The Regulators stand sentinel, wands crackling with non‑lethal voltage.

I flex my entropy‑scarred palms behind me, feeling the singe of detox burns and the new‑formed callus of resolve. The system just billed me for compassion. I’m about to invoice it for arrogance.

Hartnell steps fully inside, lips parting to speak—

[AUDIO FRAGMENT: FILE_CORRUPT_05 // SOURCE: VIRAL_FEED]

2. "Magic is Genetic"

Also wrong. Magic isn’t ancestral. Or at least, not entirely. Anyone can learn it, if they survive the process. Yes, some people are born with an edge. Sorcerers with magical, sometimes divine ancestors. Warlocks with spooky contracts. But the rest of us? We’re Wizards. We study. We train. We bleed.

Point being, you don't have to have wizard parents, sell your soul to some Otherworldy Patron, or sleep with a Satyr on a Full-Moon night to cast fancy spells. It might help, sure, but it isn't a prerequisite.

Most folks slot Magi into two neat piles: the "born with it", and "broke themselves for it". Sorcerer's and Wizards. Those born with innate magic and those who learned magic. Depending on how they use their magic, a mage might be a Warlock, Druid, Cleric - or some DIY hybrid nightmare. Labels are blurry. People aren’t spreadsheets.

*Say it with me now: "All Sorcerers, Warlocks, Druids, and Clerics are Wizards. Not all Wizards are Sorcerers, Warlocks Druids, or Clerics. More on this later.*

3. Magic is Easy.

Magic is a skill. It’s learned, refined, and brutally punishing. Most people burn out before they master their first real spell. And the ones who don’t? They still screw up. One missed calculation and your fireball becomes a localized sun. Good luck explaining that to your neighbors.

It can take years of practice, study, and application to be capable of Magic, decades for it to be useful. You ever run into a street magician? I'll bet you a haypenny you've encountered a Mage. That's right, maybe one in ten times, that guy actually pulled that card out of your ear. The sad part is that's probably as much magic as they are capable of. Poor booboo probably exhausted themself for the rest of the day to make you giggle. Magical processes require a lot of concentration. Think the same Order of Magnitude as trying to run PERL code in your head. Physically, you're literally burning calories to give physics that sweet chin music. Combine with the mental toll, and you're looking at a difficulty curve that intersects with NASA Scientists and Olympic Athletes.

4. Magic is Free

Absolutely not. The universe is a stingy bastard. Magic increases entropy - chaos. Every spell you cast pushes the universe a little closer to heat death. You can maybe reverse that with some extremely complex negentropic spells, but most mages just blow stuff up and call it a day.

In our world, this concept is known as "Equivalent Exchange." You burn what you’ve got to break the rules - heat, stamina, heartbreak, whatever’s handy. Magic doesn’t run on goodwill. If you are prepared, you can use an external source of Magic, and If you get creative enough, that cost can be reduced to next to nothing. But never "something" for "nothing".

There’s no "spell slots" or wands that do all the heavy lifting. Every magical action you take costs energy - from your body (Physical Energy), your mind (Spiritual Energy), but usually some mixture of the two. The more intricate or powerful the spell, the more you burn through your reserves.

Imagine running a marathon while solving differential equations in your head. That’s what casting a mid-level spell feels like. The higher-tier stuff? That’s Olympic-level burnout paired with a migraine and an existential crisis.

Most mages, even those of the highest order, will still find themselves catatonic trying to conjure Silver. Gold? Uranium? Platinum? Don’t even try. Nature charges interest for those. You’d need the First or Fourth Divinity, and those haven’t been seen since gods walked the earth - or pretended to.

Because no matter what you do, using magic incurs a cost. And the bill always comes due.
Wanna make a fireball? Best to hang onto that Zippo for now.

Hartnell doesn’t step closer. Doesn’t want to catch the contagion of whatever I’ve become—outlaw, martyr, PR nightmare. Instead he addresses the nearest scribe‑drone.

"Begin Session Log," he says, voice as crisp as the crease in his slacks.

A rune blooms mid‑air: Session 2114‑BX — Subject: Rao, B.

"Timestamp twenty‑two twenty." Hartnell’s gaze flicks to me. "Patient is conscious, under custodial restraint, displaying elevated entropy markers and class‑three thaumic contamination."
"Cleric," I correct, tasting blood and peroxide on my tongue. "Cleric contamination."
Hartnell’s smile is thin enough to draft through. "Thank you for your input, Mr. Rao. I’ll annotate the files accordingly once legal counsel advises."
He pivots a fraction— just enough to signal the conversation is a courtesy, not a right. "First and foremost, I trust you appreciate the severity of your misconduct."
"Oh, absolutely," I say. "What’s the etiquette for saving lives these days? Pinky up, two spoons, or just don’t bother?"
His nostrils pinch, but he keeps the veneer. "We have protocols. You violated them— with catastrophic disregard for safety. Five municipal runic grids overloaded in response to your null‑field spike. The regulator beacon registered a thaumic bloom equivalent to a class‑six battle invocation."
"Funny, because I remember it as the equivalent of ‘girl keeps breathing.’ Measurement error, maybe?"

Hartnell ignores the jab and produces a flex tablet. The hologram projects a grainy alley feed: me kneeling, runes flaring violet, regulations be damned. Audio picks up my hoarse voice crooning that nugget of the Old Speech, Burn the end into birth. A moment later, the spike—surveillance sigils spiral into static. Hartnell freezes the frame on the girl’s reanimated gasp.

"Heroic cinematography," he says, "but let’s examine consequences. Your unsupervised null‑field ruptured three municipal time‑delay wards. If a displacement inversion propagated—"
"—the block could have hosted a flash blizzard. But it didn’t. Because I anchored the field. Because someone," I jangle the cuffs, "wasn’t there to do his job."

The temperature drops a metaphorical ten degrees.

"I was summoned to an emergency quorum on resource allocation," Hartnell says, tone clipped. "We’re finalizing upgrades to the academic trauma wing, the very wing that allows you students your subsidized graveside tinkering."
"Tinkering? She was dying, Hartnell. This wasn't some foray into Medical Experimentation that I undertook out of curiosity!"
"And now the Academy’s facing an investigatory board­—Ethics, Regulation, Mana Infrastructure." Hartnell’s voice remains measured, but the veneer strains. "Do you understand what that means, Mr. Rao? Every grant, every licensure, every bed in our wards is hostage to perception. Your theatrics jeopardized more lives than you imagine."
"Perception?" The word tumbles out half‑laugh, half‑growl. "Is that what flips the healing switch? Not the Hippocratic Oath, not common decency— just whether the paperwork sparkles?" The room goes dark, save for the emergency lights. Hartnell turns to leave.
"A pity," he says softly. "You have such... capacity. But capacity without control is just a bomb waiting for a fuse."

He pauses at the threshold.

"We will garnish your assets to balance the ward expenditure, Mr. Rao. Your father's care account, the clinic lease, your omni-tool license. All of it."

My sarcasm respirator fails. The air leaves my lungs not in a quip, but a hollow rattle. For a second, I'm not a defiant cleric; I'm a son watching a ventilator screen go dark.

"Consider this mercy. We are removing the matches from your hands."
I grind my teeth through the pulse. "Systems that abandon a bleeding kid because the surgeon of record’s sipping canapés at a donors’ gala."
His eyes harden. "Watch your tone."
"Or what? You’ll what, dock my nonexistent pay? Flunk me out of a program that already affixed the target on my back?"

The door hisses open again. A pair of Regulators step in, hands resting on truncheons etched with subdual glyphs. Hartnell doesn’t acknowledge them; they’re punctuation.

He exhales. "Let me clarify the predicament. A student cleric, unauthorized, performed forbidden spellwork resulting in infrastructure damage and violation of the Mana Stability Accords. Fact. Said student’s academic record—" swipe, swipe, hologram flips to my transcripts— "notes disciplinary warnings for insubordinate commentary during Grand Rounds, breaches of curfew, possession of restricted grimoires. Pattern of defiance. Fact."

One Regulator smirks. I memorize his badge number.

"Investigators will extrapolate," Hartnell continues. "They will ask if your instructors foster radicalism. They will ask whether the Academy endorses vigilantism. The donors whose generosity keeps our wards active will ask if their names are tarnished. Do you grasp the scale?"
I stare at him, fighting the suppression field’s vertigo. My heart hammers arrhythmic, a stubborn drum corps refusing to cede tempo.
"I grasp that a girl breathes tonight. That scale fits in my palms fine."
Hartnell’s voice drops. "And if she had imploded? If that null‑field collapsed the block and turned half a district into entropy slurry?"
"Then I’d take responsibility. The difference between us? I was there to try."

The regulator behind me tugs a baton loose, perhaps anticipating we’ll skip straight to the beating portion. Hartnell lifts a hand— hold. He steps closer, finally breaching the invisible moat. I catch hints of bergamot cologne layered over something else. Not guilt. Formaldehyde and old paper.

He lowers his voice. "I recognize talent, Rao. Your early practicals were… spectacular." He says it like a diagnosis. "But raw talent without governance is a loaded wand at a toddler’s birthday. If you truly wish to heal, submit to the system. Accept censure, remedial courses, recalibration of your entropy thresholds—"
"—and let that girl die?"
"The ambulance would have reached her eventual—"
"She had three minutes."

Hartnell doesn't flinch. He doesn't look away. He looks... tired. The weariness of a man who has had this conversation a thousand times, usually with a mirror.

"Three minutes," he repeats, weighing the words. "And in exchange for those three minutes, you risked a Class IV audit. Your mother had that same disregard for variables, Bodhi."

The name hits me like a riot baton to the gut. Nalini.

"She was brilliant," Hartnell continues, voice thickening with something dangerously close to reverence. "But she never understood that a surgeon needs a shield, not just a scalpel. I didn't build this department to restrain you, Bodhi. I built it so you wouldn't have to die to make a difference. Like she did."

My vision blurs. The containment field whines as my entropy spikes.

"You don't get to throw her name in my face," I snarl. "I am not your failure, 'Lucy boy'."

Hartnell stiffens. The nickname—the one she used to tease him with in the residency lounge during double shifts—strikes him like a physical blow. For a split second, the polished mask cracks. I see outrage there, hot and messy, shadowed by a quiet, devastating guilt.

"You and the rest of her 'friends' let her burn herself out," I accuse, voice shaking. "You let her die to cover for your own mediocrities!"

"I was her student, Rao!" he snaps, the volume finally cracking the clinical hush. "I respected her more than anyone. But I watched her burn herself out on lost causes until there was nothing left for the patients who could be saved."

He steps closer, eyes pleading for the first time. "You were supposed to be the one who survived! The one who balanced the equation! Instead, you're just... her. Another beautiful, burning waste."

The math is cold. It's also correct. That makes it worse. But I look at his cufflinks—diamond-point, catching the sterile light.

"Tell me," I say, voice softer now, dangerous. " did you convince yourself of that before or after the third glass of vintage wine at the gala tonight? While you were courting cheques?"

I lean in, fighting the restraint field.

"You talk about survival, Doctor. But you look remarkably comfortable in your cage. Do you even taste the food anymore? Or does it all just taste like the compromises you swallowed to pay for it?"

Hartnell’s expression doesn't crack again. It solidifies. He looks at me not with anger, but with a terrifying, dull sympathy.

Hartnell straightens, smoothing a crease in his coat that didn't exist. "I burned like you once, Rao. You think you're the first to discover righteous indignation? It doesn't end in glory. It ends in ash or ice."

"At least ash is real," I spit.

He signals the Regulators. The moment is over.

"I chose ice," he murmurs, almost to himself. "Because ice preserves things."

One Regulator strides forward, producing a new pair of cuffs inscribed with more aggressive suppression runes.

"On your knees," he barks.

I don’t move. I hold Hartnell’s gaze.

"You chose wrong, Doctor," I say.

My eyes find his, searching for the man who must have existed before the budget meetings.

"Let it burn."

Hartnell pauses. For a second, just a second, the ice thaws enough to show the cracks underneath. Then he turns away.

"Doctor."

He pauses at the threshold.

Hartnell answers without turning. "If your hands endanger this Academy again, Mr. Rao, there won’t be enough of them left to catch a raindrop."

The door seals, swallowing him in lamplight.

The Regulators are rough but rote. Baton man yanks me free of the restraint column, clamps the new cuffs tight enough to numb. Mana‑dampening glyphs crawl over my skin like frost spiders, but I keep Hartnell’s departing silhouette etched behind my eyelids, a negative image that fuels the furnace.

They march me down a narrow service corridor smelling of coolant and despair, past frosted windows where other students pretend not to look. Each step rattles the floor grate. I catalog the route—I lift my hand again. The rune is gone. Faded to a faint, violet bruise that feels like sunburn under the skin.

"Next stop," I whisper to the silent, white room that smells of ozone and old fear. "The Tribunal." Left at the oxidized vent stack, right past the silent morgue elevator—because the practical part of my mind is already mapping escapes.

We stop at a thick quartz door stenciled Holding A‑3: High Entropy Risk. The lock‑glyph utters its sterile chirp, slides open. Inside, a cube of blank white angles waits. No runic graffiti yet— challenge accepted.

The guard shoves me just hard enough that I stumble. A swallow of laughter bubbles up; I let it out.

"Careful. I’m fragile inventory."

The door seals. Suppression wards thrummm in their endless hum. In solitude, the detox burns fade to an ache. My thoughts, unmuted, roar.

I think of the girl— unknown name, lungs full of winter air instead of blood. I think of Hartnell’s cufflinks gleaming First, do no harm while he weaponized procedure to justify inaction. I press my forehead to the cool quartz and exhale the promise I forged in violet fire.

"If the system won’t heal," I whisper, "then the system’s the illness."

Somewhere distant, a chime announces tribunal preparations. I flex my hands, even though the cuffs bite. The hunger to fix things gnaws louder than the pain.

5. "Magic is Uniform"

Feckin' laughable. Capital L.

Magic isn’t uniform. It’s messy. It's inconsistent. It’s personal. But try telling that to an academy-trained bureaucrat with a wand up their ass and a PhD in Thaumaturgy. The magical world loves its categories - Magecraft vs. Occult Practice, "High Magic" vs. "Wild Magic," formal vs. tribal. As if a hex from a back-alley Bokor is somehow less legitimate than a spell written in eleven-point Times New Roman with footnotes. They divvied up magic into "Schools" like a Hogwarts-themed TED Talk: Alchemy? Chemistry with a god complex. Potions? Middle-school chemistry, but deadly. Runic Magic? Writing that bites. Ritual Magic? Weird hats, Latin chanting, and definitely candles. Divination? Magical gambling with worse odds than Vegas. And Healing? Oh, that’s where they put us - the glorified magical nurses. You know, the ones who hold you together while everyone else is too busy blowing things up for prestige.

If you really want to get spicy, ask ten different nations what "real" magic looks like. In Vaticanus, it’s prayer. In NAMF, it’s code. In Moscow-Kiev, it’s socialist ritualism. Then you’ve got the stuff the Western world either exoticizes or ignores - Djinn-binding in Cairo, shadowcasting in Lagos, Kabbalistic syntax in Jerusalem, Tantric recursion in the Punjab Republic. These aren’t just styles. They’re entire magical ideologies, shaped by belief, geography, and power. Magic isn’t neutral - it reflects who gets to define "order" and whose gods are allowed to speak. Westerners love acting like they discovered structure, but go tell a Haitian Bokor or a Himalayan monk that their magic’s outdated. If they’re feeling generous, you’ll walk away with a minor hex. If not? Well… hope your insurance covers metaphysical trauma.

Cliff Notes on the School's of Magic
  • *Alchemy – "Chemistry with a god complex."
  • *Potions – "Middle-school chemistry, but deadly."
  • *Runic Magic – "Writing that bites."
  • *Ritual Magic – "Weird hats, chanting in dead languages, probably some candles."
  • *Spellcasting – "The 'Just Add Fireball' school."
  • *Healing Magic – "Where miracles and malpractice meet."
  • *Divination – "Basically magical gambling."
  • *Dark Magic – "Exactly what it sounds like."
  • *Chaos, Entanglement, Mind, Environmental Magic – "A catch-all for weirdos who couldn’t pick a lane."
  • *Thaumaturgy – "The science of magical bullshit."

The door seals behind me with the hiss of a scab re‑forming. No clang, no theatrical boom—just a polite exhalation of pressured air as layers of rune‑latticed crystal slide into place. A pulse slithers through the cell’s perimeter glyphs, colorless lightning mapping the walls before dwindling to a faint infrared shimmer. They’ve warded this box to within an inch of theological paranoia: null‑weave mesh in the grout, dampening runes at knee height, a suppression lattice that hums just below the threshold of conscious hearing.

I conduct the room‑check on reflex—the same checklist they drill into every Academy initiate—but the irony is a razor to the gums. Containment integrity? Immaculate. Escape vectors? None worth the entropy burn. Magical field density? Negative—beyond baseline, really; my own aura feels like it’s wearing a straightjacket three sizes too small.

I flex my fingers. Knuckles pop. The echo dies a timid death against the glassy pane that passes for a window: three centimeters of ward‑infused silicate, fogged to opacity from the other side. I see my reflection instead.

Shit. I look wrecked. Split lip, one nostril caked in dried crimson, detox scorch marks striped across my forearms like phantom cuffs. The violet flare I spotted earlier—inkless veins of light licking across my entropy wards—have faded to inert graphite, but a faint shimmer lingers when I breathe too sharply. I file that under: concerning, revisit later.

The cell is eight paces long, five paces wide— because that’s how many paces it takes before my heel clips the far bench. I pivot, pace again. Eight, five, turn. There’s a timetable hidden in the rhythm: father’s respirator beeping in memory’s ICU; mother’s heartbeat monitor stuttering to a final flatline; Academy tuition invoices piling like bad omens on our kitchen table back in the days when rent was a near‑mythic creature—always migrating, never home.

Eight, five, turn.

My breath fogs white ghosts into the chill. Condensation webs across the ward‑glass, fat droplets dragging slow tears downward—a mockery of the girl’s alveoli earlier, knitting themselves back together under my palms. A fresh jolt of anger flares at Hartnell’s lacquered indignation, at the Regulators’ lock‑step morality, at the elder board’s impending pageantry. My anger tastes of solder smoke and old pennies.

"You can’t keep me caged forever," I mutter—to the walls, the wards, the memory of Dr. Lucien Hartnell’s immaculate sneer. "But you’ll try, won’t you? Gods forbid a kid keeps breathing without a signed requisition form."

The room’s lights do not answer. They only flicker—once, like nervous laughter trapped in a filament.
My perception shifts.

It starts as the high‑frequency buzz of the suppression lattice but morphs—subtly, inexorably—into the whine of ventilators. I’m no longer pacing a holding cell; I’m back in St. Gabrielle’s Medical Wing, three years prior, fluorescent bulbs bleaching everything to corpse colors.

Dad lies swaddled in thrumming machinery. An arcane respirator—part machine, part bound elementals—forces each rise and fall of his chest. Respiratory Failure Type IV. A cleric from the Academy briefed us on prognosis in the dulcet tones reserved for terminal cases: "Stable as long as the mana subsidy lasts." Translation: Stable until you can’t pay.

Mom signed the release forms with shaking hands. Their ink smudged; her knuckles did the same. We spent nights counting medical crystals, recalculating how many cubic centimeters of regulated miracle we could afford.

I remember kissing Dad’s forehead, tasting antiseptic and fear. I remember whispering a promise: I’ll learn, Dad. I’ll fix this.

The memory fractures—

—reassembling into a bursar’s office lined with floating ledger‑orbs. Numbers scroll along their mirrored surfaces, each digit another centimeter of noose. Scholarships? Conditional. Loans? Predatory. My acceptance letter glowed like salvation until the addendum spelled out the price of each "authorized" miracle: Fifty‑two thousand credits per annum, reagent cost not included.

Mom sold the house. The respirator bill swelled anyway. I deferred a semester, took two jobs—graveyard shifts at a mana refinery and midnight runs in a trauma clinic triage bay where license checks were more optional suggestion than rule. That’s where I learned to hack the Academy limiters—small tweaks at first, life or death tweaks eventually.

Back in present, my pacing falters. A metallic tang pricks behind my teeth—anger, adrenaline, or just poor ventilation. Hard to tell.

Condensation on the glass sharpens into an ICU window again, but this one belongs to Mom. Pneumonic Magic Burn—complication from a spell she cast to save a child trapped in a flood. Unauthorized thaumaturgy, no protective null field. She drowned from the inside while regulators filed negligence charges.

I visited during the "family hours" they grant right before final collapse. Mom pressed a shaking finger to my chest. "Power is only sacred when it’s spent lifting another soul. So reach—any hour, any street—and take their weight, even if the world never thanks you. Let purpose pay the cost gratitude can’t. And when your own flame gutters, make sure the room is crowded with hearts you’ve carried. Don’t slip away lonely like your father and me." The monitor silenced before I could answer. The curtain between us and everything righteous fell.

Back to the claustrophobic present.

I stop pacing. I will not be her martyr. I’ve bled tuition, sweat, tears, and enough raw entropy to power a small city, but I will not be a footnote mislabeled reckless.

I wipe away the hanging beads of condensation, clearing a narrow patch on the ward‑glass. My breath fogs it again, a fresh canvas. With the tip of my index, I draw—slow, deliberate lines: a downward hook, two crossing slashes, a spiral folding in on itself. It’s the entropy sigil they teach as cautionary lore in Intro to Magical Thermodynamics—the symbol you should never evoke without stabilizers, because it invites chaos to dinner and forgets to set the table.

My finger glows faintly as I trace. The ward‑glass sizzles where skin meets silicate, but no alarms scream. The suppression lattice shivers, unsure whether to stop me—this gesture is too small, too symbolic to quantify as a violation.

The spiral completes. A tremor ripples through my bones—like a tuning fork struck in the marrow. The sigil hums a note I feel more than hear, resonant with the latent violet flicker buried under my skin. It’s not magic, not exactly. It’s intention. Intent shapes resonance; resonance becomes potential.

"There’s a cost for every miracle," I whisper, voice rasping the cell’s recycled air. "So charge me. I’m done bartering with innocent lives."

I press my palm flat against the sigil. The cold eats through the calluses. Memory and present overlap until I’m holding my father’s hand, clutching my mother’s, stabilizing a dying stranger, all at once.

A single tear escapes—annoyingly sentimental—but I don’t wipe it. It crawls to my jaw, drops, splashes on the rune‑scored floor. It hisses where it lands. The lattice dims fractionally; the system registers an anomaly but not enough to mobilize.

I breathe, count to five, release.

My life until tonight has been scaffolding—courses, clinical rotations, sanctioned mission trips to disaster zones where every act of compassion came with a compliance checklist. That scaffolding is ash now. What I did in the alley was the bare minimum of what a cleric should do, yet it shattered the facade. The board will brand me reckless. Hartnell will spin a narrative where his absence is procedural necessity and my intervention is criminal zealotry.

Fine.

I step back from the sigil. It glows faintly on the glass, pulsing with each heartbeat—mine, the girl’s, Mom’s, Dad’s—a little constellation of debts.

In the distant corridor, a klaxon bleats: two throaty wails and a long drone. That’s the signal for transfer— they’re moving detainees to pre‑trial holding. My time in this box is up.

I straighten. Shake the stiffness from my shoulders. The sigil fades—the condensation bead‑trails evaporating as if ashamed. But the imprint stays branded behind my eyelids.

Eight paces to the door. I’m halfway across when the rune locks unseal with a percussive clack. The door slides, revealing two Regulators in muted crimson, their batons crackling with polite menace.

"Rao, Bodhi. Transfer protocol," the taller one states, visor reflecting my ghost‑lit face.

I nod, wrists offered. The cuffs are colder than before, or maybe I’ve just run out of body heat to lose.

As they guide me into the corridor, I glance back. The humid patch on the window is gone. Only a faint outline—less visible than a fingerprint—remains where the entropy spiral once burned.

But I know it’s there. Like a promise sworn in blood and cold flame.

The Regulators march me toward an elevator caged in thaumic mesh. Beyond the grate, the shaft descends deeper—toward tribunal chambers, public scrutiny, and the choreography of institutional judgment. I roll my shoulders, composing arguments sharper than scalpels and jokes darker than the shaft’s abyss.

Next stop: the Tribunal of Ivory Smiles.
Let them bare their teeth. I’ve already tasted worse.

Chapter 3

Chapter 3: Tribunal of Ivory Smiles

[AUDIO FRAGMENT: FILE_CORRUPT_07 // SOURCE: VIRAL_FEED]

BODHI'S LIST OF THINGS PEOPLE GET WRONG ABOUT MAGIC (Cont'd)

6. Magic is Unifying

Magic’s just belief that bites back. And like belief, it’s never neutral - it’s loaded, cultural, political, and pissed off. Magic allowed Mages to turn gods into mana batteries, worshipping into spell prep.

Let me guess - you thought magic had no borders. That it was some sort of universal force, accessible to all, pure and egalitarian, like sunlight or gravity or Spotify Premium. Cute. The reality? The world’s been chopped into 77 magical nation-states, each one with its own spells, gods, taxes, and geopolitical tantrums. Magic isn’t free - it’s franchised. And trust me, the global magical order runs about as smoothly as a three-legged centaur on cobblestones. The most powerful states - Vaticanus, Avalon, Nihon - hoard relics like dragons and rewrite the rules of reality from gilded parliamentary sanctums while the rest of us sweat through spellwork for minimum enchantment. Magical privilege is real. Healers like me? We’re the magical working class. We pour our lifeblood into mending wounds while some state-sponsored Illuminatus in a runic robe gets tenure for levitating spoons.

I live in the North Atlantic Magic Federation, or NAMF - and no, the acronym doesn’t get any cooler with time. Imagine if Silicon Valley went to Burning Man, read three books on Indigenous spirituality, then tried to run a government. That’s NAMF. A hyper-technocratic patchwork of mana grids, bureaucratic mysticism, and chakra-branding startups with names like "SoulSync." They gentrified magic. Here, shamanic rituals get repackaged as "neural field recalibration experiences," and ancestral spells are copyrighted by hedge-funded AlchemTech firms. It’s appropriation with an expense report. And the worst part? They think they fixed magic - standardized it, regulated it, made it safe for public use. But they didn’t. They just made it palatable to the colonizers. Every nation practices its own flavor of the arcane - from Celtic Druidry to Zoroastrian Firecraft - but NAMF acts like it invented the wheel because it gave it Bluetooth. Meanwhile, they still ignore the fact that the borders of these magical states were drawn with blood, theft, and bureaucratic quills by the same empires that once stole gods and named them "myths." Magic isn’t a gift. It’s a history. And in this world, history’s got landlords.

The corridor leading to the tribunal looks like it was carved by someone who hated angles. Every surface sweeps in soft cathedral arches—no corners for paranoia to roost, no shadows deep enough to hide contrition. Runic sconces glow in sterile amber, their light filtered through suppression glyphs that taste of copper on the tongue. Two Regulators flank me, batons cradled in practiced indifference. My cuffs have been replaced by a braided chain-hex—pretty, platinum, horrendously expensive. It hums against my wrist with the smug tenor of old money.

Note to self: Invoice the Council for accessorizing. Labor isn’t cheap.

Ahead, the doors loom. Twin titans of rune-laced lunastone, inlaid with the Academy’s crest—Serpens Ouroboros devouring its tail around a stylized caduceus. The message is simple: everything feeds the institution eventually, even itself. As I cross the threshold a chill sluices down my spine, equal parts climate control and psychological conditioning. I step onto obsidian that reflects me in broken prisms—fractured Bodhi multiplied, each shard a different mistake. The room smells of ozone and the gut-hollow lightness of huge, empty spaces designed to make you feel small.

The chamber proper is a vertical coliseum flipped inside‑out. Instead of spectators, tiered balconies hold mosaic panels depicting legendary miracles: Saint Torin closing plague‑sores with a hymn; High‑Cherurg Tenebris suturing reality to stop a dimensional bleed. All executed in luminous tesserae that shift color when you blink. I count twelve elders stationed around a horseshoe dais, each perched behind a half‑moon pulpit of orichalcum lattice. Their robes are winter‑storm gray, a color patented by the Academy to denote unassailable authority™.

Suspension wards shimmer overhead—concentric discs of frozen starlight rotating at contemplative speed. They leach magic from the air in lazy siphons, the way bored aristocrats drain champagne flutes. My tattoos prickle under the drag, entropy wards sighing like exhausted lungs.

A clerk in waist-coat and levitating quill reads charges that dance holographically between us: UNLICENSED MIRACLE-WORKING. RECKLESS THAUMATURGY. DAMAGE TO MUNICIPAL WARDS. Then the sub-headers scroll, the fine print that actually ruins you: Immediate Licensure Revocation. Retroactive Debt Bondage for Academy Tuition. Permanent Blacklisting from NAMF Territories. The words crackle, then evaporate into faint motes that settle on my shoulders like ash.

"Defendant, state your full name for the record."
"State it? Note the record says 'Defendant,' so let's save time. Bodhisunya Rao. Friends call me Bodhi. You can call me 'The Reason Your Grid Didn't Melt'."

A ripple of disapproval races the balconies. Good—wouldn’t want anyone getting comfortable. Looking up at the "Council of Porcelain Masks", I scan the horseshoe dais for the Primary Speakers.

Center stage is Arch-Elder Virelia Mournwright, skin like bleached parchment stretched over old fury, nose pinched by pince-nez that double as a rune scanner. To her right sits Elder Magistrate Cleon Astell, financier turned ethicist, his robe pin adorned with the sigil of the Chamber of Commerce because even hypocrisy needs branding. And on the far left... Elder-Healer Saanvi Belgaran. The only genuine clinician present. Her eyes are rimmed crimson—sleepless nights or suppressed empathy, hard to tell.

The rest form an equal‑opportunity mural of disdain—administrators, philanthropist backers, two politicians who arrived late and smell faintly of after‑banquet mead.

Seeing Elder Saanvi sitting amongst the Judiciaries causes my throat to tighten. The guilt hits me then—not a feeling, but a physical weight, like swallowing a stone. Saanvi was not only a personal mentor of mine at the Academy, but my mother's best friend. Disappointing her wasn't on the agenda.

Virelia leans forward, hands steepled.

"Mr. Rao, the Council appreciates your… enthusiasm for patient safety. However, your methodology raises grave concerns. You performed a Class IV Sub-Resurrection Causal Inversion within an unsanctioned Null Field. Alone."
"Correct. I also didn't wait for a committee to vote on gravity while the kid fell. Minor miracle that I managed to avoid spilling coffee on my white coat. If we're listing irrelevant facts."
Cleon’s jaw flexes. "Flippancy will not serve you here."
"Yeah, well neither will appeasement."

Saanvi interjects, voice brittle but weighted with something heavier than annoyance.

"The Entropy indices recorded a seventeen‑point escalation. You claim to have controlled a Class 4 Paradox with willpower, Bodhi. That is not science; it is delusion. The Ward Lattice was already destabilized by the Lunar transit. Your 'miracle' nearly triggered a cascade failure across the entire eastern seaboard. You didn't just risk yourself. You risked everyone."
I nod, the humor draining out of my posture. "I know the math, Saanvi. But the math doesn't scream when it bleeds out."

A few students in the upper mezzanine—allowed to observe for pedagogical value—stifle laughs before tutors glare them silent.

Virelia raps a gavel of moon‑silver. The sound is soft yet final, like a guillotine cushioned with velvet.

"Explain your spellwork. In detail."

I unspool it. How Standard Regeneration forces cellular genesis forward—too slow for a pulmo‑hemorrhagic collapse. How Time Reversal bends causality but drags surrounding reality like a toddler’s blanket, risking wide‑area paradox. How both require umpteen practitioners chanting in key while the patient’s life bleeds out a metronome. My method? Entropy drag.

"You treat injuries as open thermodynamic systems," I say, pacing within my hex‑radius. "Force the wound state to trade entropy with the Null Field. Body moves toward lower‑entropy configuration—aka healthy—while the field uptakes the disorder. The trick is establishing a fuse."
Cleon sneers. "Spare us the poetry, Mr. Rao. The actuarial tables don't care about your 'tricks'. The Null Field collapsed twenty‑three seconds post‑procedure."
"Because I was the fuse, Cleon. If the field collapsed, it would have imploded me, not the ward. I did the math. I just didn't factor in your comfort levels. Zero civilian casualties, unless you count my career. But that was terminal anyway."
Astell turns plum. "Irrelevant. You circumvented every safety protocol to flaunt your—"
"—to save a kid abandoned by protocol," I cut in. My voice echoes, riding the chamber’s perfect acoustics. "Had I waited for your four‑thaumiphysician choir, she’d be a chalk outline."

Murmurs bubble. Virelia raises her hand and the room stills.

"Intent does not absolve recklessness. Doctrine exists to harness power for the common good."
I laugh, short and crooked. "Doctrine exists to gatekeep the divine. If good were common we wouldn’t price it by the hour."

A sub‑clerk hovers a scroll‑slab toward me. Case histories flicker: Alcester v. The Collective, Van‑Luik Reversal Incident, each a cautionary tale of lone mages who cooked themselves alive attempting entropy inversion. My favorite bedtime stories.

Virelia taps the scroll. "These tragedies prove why quorum is mandatory."

"They prove why the quorum of cowards is lethal," I reply. "Every one of those med‑teams refused to act until they formed a committee. People died waiting for a vote that never closed."

Saanvi’s eyes flutter shut. She’s seen the same corpses. Cleon arches a brow, scenting weakness in his colleague.

"Elder Belgaran, perhaps you’d like to remind the defendant of the stable miracles ratio?"
She exhales. "Three practitioners minimize entropy feedback by distributing metabolic cost across synced circulatory matrices—"
"—Unless one practitioner is me," I interrupt. "My vitals remain within tolerance post‑spell." I hold out my hands, palms up. "No necrotic bloom. No fissured aura. Check the telemetry."

A holographic panel materializes mid‑air, courtesy of the Regulator medic who processed me. My biosign curves glide across in neat sine waves. Heart rate elevated but steady; entropy saturation at 62%—ugly but survivable.

Saanvi frowns at the data, torn between disbelief and grudging respect. Cleon overrides.

"Even if you withstood it, you jeopardized the ward lattice. We had to divert power from neonatal stasis rooms to reinforce the grid. Three infants nearly de-synced because you wanted to play god with a gunshot wound."

I bite back a retort about billable priorities. Instead I pivot.

"The patient lives."

The statement hangs like incense. No one responds. Truth rarely needs allies; it just needs air.

A hush falls so complete I swear I hear dust motes pirouette. In that pause my gaze drifts upward, past suppression discs, to stained‑glass oculi depicting The First Miracle: a robed figure splitting darkness with light. I wonder what tribunal he faced afterward.

Heat stings behind my eyes—unexpected, unwelcome. I channel it into words.

"My mother died in a ward ten meters from here," I say softly. "Tumor the size of regret in her frontal lobe. Academy surgeons called it economically non‑viable to attempt Class III excision. You signed that letter, Arch‑Elder."

Virelia blinks once, the only crack in her alabaster mask.

"You conflate policy with—"
"I conflate policy with hypocrisy." The admission tastes like rust. "So when a girl rolled into that alley with her lungs sautéed, I saw my mother’s ghost telling me to break the rules that broke her."

No one meets my eyes. Good. Let guilt crowd their retinas.

Elder Technocrat Imir Solvan, silent until now, adjusts his neural halo—thin ring of light mapping mind to archive.

"Mr. Rao, your incantation contained an anomalous glyphic sub-routine: ThRun-kel Tuu Vaar'lenna. Translation lost to public lexicons. Where did you obtain it?"

Ah, there it is—the real hunt.

"My grandmother’s prayer book," I lie smoothly. "A bedtime benediction for unruly weather. Old family superstition."

The lie settles heavy in my gut. I’m not just hiding a technique; I’m hiding Her. If they knew Saint Nalini was dabbling in Atavistic Entropy—messing with forces that could unzip the city—her statue in the atrium would be rubble by morning.

Imir’s halo stutters. He knows I’m bluffing. The ring of light abruptly contracts, tightening around his temples like a vice, casting a targeting reticle over his left eye. He leans forward, the light burning hot enough to smell like ozone. He knows. But proving it would require a deep-dive scan that overrides tribunal protocol. He slowly relaxes the halo, accepting the convenient fiction, but the threat remains scorched into the air. Better a reckless student than a corrupted legend.

Virelia signals the dais. A translucent veil descends, muting their voices while they deliberate. From my position I watch their silhouettes mime outrage, concern, self‑preservation—an interpretive dance of bureaucracy.

It hits me then, watching them argue not about if I should be expelled, but how. They aren't expelling me because I failed. They're expelling me because I worked. Because I used "impossible" magic to fix a problem their perfect System declared unsolvable. If I can rewrite entropy without their permission, what else can I do? I am a variable they cannot isolate, an irregularity in their perfect equation.

Above, suppression discs slow, as if the room itself holds its breath.

Minutes stretch origami-long. Students whisper; quills scratch; somewhere a regulator pops gum. I roll my shoulders. The hex-chain loosens a millimeter—enough to remind me entropy favors change. I could snap it, maybe. Melt through the suppression grid, sprint for the corridor. But that would undermine the point: the guilty should not need to run.

The veil rises.

Virelia stands, gavel poised but not yet struck.

"The Council finds the defendant—"

A hush funnels every heartbeat into my ears. I see Saanvi’s lips tremble, Cleon’s nostrils flare, Imir’s halo spin.

"—in violation of Articles 7, 12, and 19. Sentencing to be pronounced following procedural ratification by the Internal Safety Review Board at oh‑six‑hundred."

So they kick the can to sunrise. Bureaucracy’s favorite alarm clock.
Cleon adds, "In the interim, you remain in custody under probationary suspension."

Regulators move to escort me. I smirk.

"Guess I’m trending."
