I sobbed for the thief, the monster, and the god who was currently crying in a pile of garbage because he’d finally performed a miracle, and it was a punchline.

Why did it always have to end like this?

Chapter 10

Chapter 10: Ultimatum at Midnight

Scene 56

The Cavaretta Estate didn't just sit on the hill; it loomed, a Victorian tumor of brick and ivy metastasizing from the damp earth of New Canaan.

I stepped out of the taxi—the driver didn't want to come up the driveway, and I didn't blame him—and my boots crunched onto the imported Venetian gravel. The sound was too loud in the silence, a percussive announcement that Bodhi Rao, the unlicensed miracle worker, the "containment specialist," the fraud, had arrived to perform his final trick.

November mist clung to the grounds like gauze on a festering wound, dampening the sound but prioritizing the smell. And god, did it smell. Not just the pine sap and woodsmoke of the surrounding woods, but something sweeter, thicker. The cloying scent of money sweating out its toxins. It smelled like expensive cigars and old blood, a boutique abattoir for the gentry.

I adjusted my collar, a useless gesture against the chill that was radiating from the obsidian ring pressed against my sternum.

The ring.

I didn't remember putting it on. I just remembered the weight. A week? Seems longer. It felt heavy enough to bruise bone—neutron star density packed into a loop that should have weighed ounces. It pulsed against my skin, a hot, scorching rhythm that didn't match my heartbeat. Thum-thum... thum. Like a signal struggling to find a carrier wave.

Diagnostic scan, I told myself, slipping into the clinical detachment that had kept me sane through three years of back-alley surgeries. Assess the patient. Ignore the prognosis.

I looked at the house. My "Eye of Ajna"—the diagnostic sight I usually reserved for failing organs—flickered open without a conscious command. The world desaturated. The brickwork faded to grey wireframes, and the mist dissolved into data.

The estate was screaming.

Thaumic wards wrapped the structure in layers of suffocating complexity—ancient Roman protection circles spliced with modern security glyphs, a sloppy, brute-force architecture that leaked mana like a sieve. It was "Spaghetti Code" of the worst kind, generations of paranoid sorcerers patching over each other’s work until the original intent was buried under centuries of cruft.

"Amateurs," I whispered, the word smoking in the cold air.

The Blood-black Ouroboros stirred in my marrow. It wasn't a spell I had prepared; it was a parasite I was hosting. A subsonic vibration that rattled my teeth, a hunger waiting for a menu.

And the ring... the ring was hungry too.

As I crossed the boundary of the wards, the pressure against my chest spiked. But it wasn't pushing back. It was inhaling. The obsidian inlay drank the frequency of the barrier, a "Mana Sink" swallowing the energy like a black hole eating a star. The wards didn't flicker; they just... dimmed. A localized brown-out in the magical architecture, centered entirely on my sternum.

Easy, I thought at the snake, and the stone. Dinner is coming. Don't spoil your appetite.

I walked toward the mahogany double doors, my hands thrust deep into my pockets to hide the tremor. I wasn't shaking from fear. I was shaking because I was holding a grenade with the pin pulled, and the explosion was the only exit strategy I had left.

The doors swung inward before I could knock.

Bianco Cavaretta stood in the violently lit foyer, a silhouette cut from anxiety and expensive fabric. He looked younger than the last time I’d seen him, shrinking inside a suit that cost more than my father’s life insurance policy. His eyes were wide, rimmed with the red exhaustion of a man who sleeps with one eye on the exit.

"You came," he whispered. His voice was a thread, vibrating with the high-frequency terror of a prey animal caught in the lights.

I stepped across the threshold, the warmth of the foyer hitting me like a physical blow. "I'm a man of my word, Bianco. Even when the word is a lie."

He flinched, glancing toward the east wing where voices—loud, confident, dangerous—echoed off the vaulted ceiling. "My uncle... he thinks you're a specialist. A 'Containment Architect' from the Vatican underground."

"I know what he thinks," I said, my voice flat. "I wrote the script."

Bianco swallowed, his Adam’s apple bobbing. He looked at my chest, seemingly drawn by the invisible gravity of the ring hidden beneath my shirt. For a second, his terror broke, replaced by a jagged confusing. "You feel... wrong. Bodhi, the air around you is... heavy."

"That's just the luggage," I said, brushing past him. "Take me to the prep room. Before I lose my nerve."

Scene 57

Bianco led me through a side corridor that smelled of formaldehyde and lavender—the specific, nauseating perfume of a funeral home trying to pass as a spa.

The "Preparation Anteroom" was a glorified pantry, its shelves lined not with food for the living but with snacks for the dead. Glass jars filled with amber fluid held things that might have been organs, or might have been alchemical mistakes. I saw a preserved hand with six fingers, a heart that seemed to be made of quartz, and a row of canaries that were motionless but definitely not dead.

"He tests on them," Bianco said, catching my stare. "The Shard... it hums. It hurts my teeth. It ate the bird last week. It didn't kill it, Bodhi. The bird just... stopped updating. Like a frozen screen."

I froze, my hand halfway to the ceremonial robe draped over a chair. Stopped updating.

"Show me," I said.

Bianco pointed to the third jar. Inside, the canary hung suspended in the fluid, its wings spread mid-flight. But looking closer, the feathers weren't blurred by motion. They were pixelated. The edges of the wings dissolved into tiny, perfect squares of yellow light.

"Glitch," I murmured. The diagnosis was immediate and sickening. "The local reality rendering engine can't process the object state."

Bianco looked at me like I was speaking Aramaic. "What?"

"Nothing." I grabbed the robe—heavy black silk embroidered with silver thread that felt greasy to the touch. "Your uncle is playing with a debugger he doesn't understand."

I stripped off my jacket, folding it neatly on a workbench stained with old reagents. I transferred the ring from my shirt pocket to a hidden fold in the robe's inner lining, placing it directly over my heart. The warmth of the stone bled through the fabric, deeper than skin, settling into the intercostal muscles like a heated stone massage gone wrong.

"Why are you helping him?" I asked, watching Bianco’s reflection in the glass of the specimen jar. "You know he’s going to kill Vincenzo. You know this isn't a cure."

Bianco hunched his shoulders, looking suddenly like a child. "Because he's family. And because... if I don't help, I'm next."

"Self-preservation," I nodded, clinical. "The most honest diagnostic there is."

"And you?" Bianco turned, his eyes searching my face for a savior he wouldn't find. "Why are you here? You barely know us. You hate magic."

I paused, my thumb tracing the outline of the obsidian ring through the silk. The surface felt sleek, friction-free, like touching a hole in the world.

Because my father is currently a zip file on a hard drive I stole from death, I thought. And your uncle built the only computer powerful enough to unzip him.

"I'm here for a second opinion," I said aloud. "And to make sure no one else gets 'deleted' tonight."

The lie tasted like copper. I wasn't here to save anyone. I was here to hijack a ritual. If saving Bianco was a byproduct, great. If not... well, triage is a bitch.

I pulled the hood up. "Let's go. Don't keep the monster waiting."

Scene 58

The drawing room was an arena masked as a lounge.

Three mages stood in a loose semi-circle around the fireplace, their postures stiff, their eyes flicking toward the door as we entered. I clocked them instantly—"Licensed" practitioners. You could tell by the way they held themselves: arrogant but leashed. They wore the Empire's seal like a badge of honor, unaware it was a barcode.

Marco Cavaretta sat in a wingback chair that looked like a throne, swirling a glass of liquid that was too dark to be wine. He looked up, his face a landscape of sharp angles and expensive skin treatments.

"The Specialist," Marco said. His voice was a deep, resonant baritone—a voice trained to give orders that ended lives. "You're late."

"Precision is never late," I said, channeling every ounce of the sardonic, burnout medic persona I’d curated. "It arrives exactly when the variables align. Or would you prefer I rushed the containment fields and turned your estate into a crater?"

Marco smiled, but it didn't reach his eyes. His eyes were dead things, shark-black and empty. "I prefer results. These are your battery packs." He gestured loosely to the three mages.

One of them—a silver-haired woman with the sharp, predatory gaze of a corporate lawyer—stepped forward. "We are conduits, Mr. Cavaretta. Not batteries. Results require respect."

Mistake, I flagged it instantly. Never negotiate with a tumor.

Marco didn't even blink. "You are what I pay you to be. And tonight, you are input."

I walked past them, ignoring the glares. My attention was fixed on the holographic schematic floating above the coffee table—a blueprint of the ritual circle.

I scanned it with my diagnostic eye.

Energy Input: 4 Sources.
Throughput Capacity: Unlimited.
Filter Protocols: Disabled.
Safety Margins: None.

My blood ran cold. The math was wrong. Explicitly, aggressively wrong.

"Four sources?" I asked, pointing at the glowing nodes. "You have three mages."

Marco took a sip of his drink. "And you, Mr. Rao. The Specialist must anchor the field, correct? That requires... connection."

I looked at the mages. They weren't just conduits. They were fuel. And so was I. Marco didn't intend to "channel" the Philosopher's Stone to heal his brother. He intended to crack the Shard open and feed it four souls to jumpstart his own immortality.

It wasn't a surgery. It was a harvest.

"Standard protocol," I lied, keeping my voice steady even as my adrenal gland dumped a cocktail of fight-or-flight into my bloodstream. "But I'll need proximity to the Shard. To... modulate the frequency."

"Granted," Marco stood up. The air in the room seemed to drop five degrees. "We go down now. Vincenzo is fading."

I glanced at the corner, where a medical stretcher sat in the shadows. Vincenzo Cavaretta—the brother, the excuse for all this madness—was a husk. Skeletal, grey, barely breathing. He wasn't a patient; he was a prop.

Triage status: Black Tag, I noted. Unsalvageable.

"After you," I said to Marco.

Scene 59

The briefing was short, brutal, and entirely for show.

As we walked, Marco explained the "phases" of the ritual, using words like "harmonics" and "resonance" that he clearly didn't understand. It was cargo-cult alchemy—he was mimicking the motions of power without understanding the mechanics.

"The Shard requires a primer," Marco said as we reached the heavy iron door of the cellar. "A spark to open the aperture. That's where you come in, Rao. Your... unique talent for void-magic. You open the door. They—" he pointed to the mages, "—keep it open. I step through."

He knows, I realized. He knows I'm a Void-Cleric. Or at least, he knows I'm not a standard mage.

My hand strayed to my chest, pressing against the ring through the robe. The warmth was intensifying. It wasn't just heat anymore; it was mass. The ring felt like it weighed fifty pounds.

Null Variable, I thought. That’s the play.

Marco's ritual was built on a closed loop: 4 Inputs -> Shard -> Marco.
But he was assuming standard inputs. Clean mana. Licensed souls. Compliant variables.

He wasn't accounting for a Null Variable. A variable that equals zero. Or negative infinity.

If I fed the Shard the ring—if I fed it the Null Variable—it wouldn't be like pouring gas on a fire. It would be like pouring concrete into an engine.

The "System" of the Shard would try to process the data I had compressed into the obsidian. But the data wasn't just raw mana. It was complex. Healed. Condensed.

Buffer Underrun, the hacker part of my brain whispered. Feed the system a file it can't read, and watch the kernel panic.

I wasn't killing him. I was migrating him.

"You're quiet, Specialist," Marco said, pausing with his hand on the door latch.

"Just calculating safety margins," I said.

"There are no safety margins," Marco grinned, and for a second, I saw the madness underneath the suit. "That's why I hired you."

He opened the door.

Scene 60

The descent was less like walking into a basement and more like lowering yourself into a reactor core.

The stairs were stone, slick with condensation that smelled of iron and ozone. The air grew colder with every step, a biting, unnatural chill that ignored the fabric of my robe and went straight for the marrow.

Server Room temperature, I noted. Keep the hardware cool.

We emerged into the wine cellar, but the wine had been evicted. The space had been gutted, the floor lowered, and the walls reinforced with lead plating.

In the center of the room, suspended in a magnetic containment field that hummed with a headache-inducing whine, was the Shard.

I had expected a crystal. A gem. Something beautiful.

It wasn't.

It was a Dead Pixel.

A jagged, absolute black hole in the air, roughly the size of a human heart. It didn't reflect light; it drank it. The floodlights Marco had rigged up around the circle seemed to bend toward it, their beams warping and vanishing into the void of the object.

Looking at it made my eyes water. My brain tried to reject what it was seeing, filling in the gap with static. It was an object that violated the rendering rules of the universe.

"Beautiful," Marco whispered.

"It's a glitch," I said, my voice barely audible.

"It's power," Marco corrected. He turned to the mages. "Positions."

The mages took their places on three points of the etched runic circle—North, East, West.

"South," Marco pointed to the spot directly in front of the Shard. "Specialist."

I walked to the South point. The proximity to the Shard was nauseating. I could hear it now—not a hum, but a whisper. A thousand voices overlapping, screaming, crying, begging.

Unfiltered soul-data, I realized with a jolt of horror. This isn't a battery. It's a hard drive. A storage device for everyone it's ever eaten.

I took my place. I looked at Bianco, who was standing by the wall, trembling.

Get ready to run, kid, I projected the thought, hoping he could read my posture.

"Begin," Marco commanded.

Scene 61

The mages began to chant.

It wasn't Latin. It wasn't Enochian. It was pure, guttural noise—a harmonic frequency designed to resonate with the Shard.

The effect was instantaneous.

The Shard pulsed. The Dead Pixel expanded.

And the pain hit.

It wasn't pain like a cut or a burn. It was extraction. I felt a hook sink into my chest—not my physical chest, but the concept of "Me." It snagged my vitality, my mana, the very electricity keeping my synapses firing, and it pulled.

I gasped, my knees buckling. I saw the silver-haired mage to my left scream silently, her eyes rolling back as red mist began to vent from her pores.

It's eating us, I thought, panic flaring. It's not waiting for the primer. It's just hungry.

"Hold it!" Marco roared, stepping toward the circle. "Stabilize the flow!"

"I... can't..." the male mage on the East point choked out. His skin was turning grey, aging decades in seconds. "It's... too... heavy..."

The Ouroboros in my bones woke up.

It felt the threat—the predation—and it reacted with the hostility of an apex predator defending its territory. Black lightning crackled down my arms, visible even in the dim light. The Entropic Divinity didn't want to be eaten. It wanted to eat.

Not yet, I gritted my teeth, forcing the Ouroboros down. Wait for the opening.

I looked at the Shard. The aperture was widening. I could see swirls of gold and violet inside the black—the raw data of consumed souls.

"Now!" Marco screamed at me. "Open it fully, Specialist! Or I bury you here!"

I looked at him. I looked at the Shard.

"You want it open?" I snarled, my voice sounding like grinding stones.

I reached into my robe.

Scene 62

My hand closed around the ring.

It burned. Cold fire. The sensation of touching dry ice.

I ripped it from the hidden pocket. The movement was sluggish, like fighting through deep water. The Shard's gravity was distorting time, slowing down my motor functions.

Marco saw the ring. His eyes widened. "What is that? That's not the focus!"

"This," I shouted, holding the obsidian band up to the Dead Pixel, "is the audit!"

I didn't cast a spell. I didn't verify the syntax. I just jammed the ring directly into the stream of energy flowing from the mages into the Shard.

I inserted the Null Variable.

The moment the ring touched the energy stream, the world paused.

Literally.

The sound cut out. The flickering lights froze. The dust motes in the air stopped moving.

Buffer Underrun, my mind supplied.

The Shard tried to process the new input. It expected pure mana. It got a complex, encrypted, paradoxical Anomaly wrapped in sixteen hours of Entropic alteration. It got a file format it couldn't read.

Null_Variable.obj incompatible with Cavaretta_Ritual.exe.

The pause lasted for a heartbeat that felt like an hour.

Then the crash report arrived.

Scene 63

The sound returned not as a noise, but as a shockwave.

CRACK.

The air shattered.

The Shard signaled its distress by inverting the room's gravity. I was thrown upward, slamming into the ceiling—which was now the floor? No, direction was gone.

"What did you do?!" Marco screamed, his voice distorted, sounding like a digital file played at half speed. "WHAT DID YOU DO?!"

The silver-haired mage exploded. She didn't die; she just turned into a cloud of red pixels and vanished. The other two mages collapsed, their bodies twisting into impossible geometries as the Shard tried to source more RAM to process the error.

"Bianco!" I yelled, searching the chaos.

I saw him. He was sliding across the floor—or the wall—falling toward the Shard. The gravity well was dragging him in.

No, I thought. Not the kid.

My Divinity lunged.

It wasn't a choice. It was a reflex. The Blood-black Ouroboros lashed out from my shadow, a tendril of absolute negation. It didn't grab Bianco; that would have crushed him.

It struck the space next to him.

I deleted the coordinates between Bianco and the door.

Pop.

Space folded. One second Bianco was falling toward the black hole; the next, he flickered and reappeared at the top of the stairs, outside the ritual boundary. He tumbled onto the landing, safe.

"Go!" I screamed, though I doubt he could hear me over the roaring of reality tearing itself apart.

He looked back once, terrified, and then scrambled up the stairs.

Good. Triage complete.

Now for the patient.

I turned back to the Shard. It was pulsing violently now—Red. Black. Gold. Red. Black. Gold. It was choking on my father.

Marco was trying to hold it together. He was pouring his own magic into the circle, shouting command phrases that were dissolving into static. He didn't realize it was over. He was trying to debug a system that was already burning the hardware.

"It's a bad line of code, Marco!" I shouted, bracing myself against a pillar that was slowly turning into sand. "You can't fix it!"

Scene 64

The ritual circle etched into the floor began to peel away, rising into the air like ribbons of burning tape.

The "Reality Rendering" of the room was failing. Section by section, the textures were dropping out. The stone wall behind Marco turned into a grey wireframe mesh. The stretcher holding Vincenzo flickered and became a transparent polygon.

This isn't magic, I realized with a calm that terrified me. This is simulation theory weaponized.

The Shard wasn't just breaking the ritual; it was deleting the save file.

Marco finally stopped chanting. He looked at his hands. They were translucent. He could see his own bones, glowing with the golden light of the mana he'd hoarded.

He looked at me. For the first time, the arrogance was gone. There was only the confusion of a user who clicked 'Save' and saw 'File Corrupted'.

"You..." he whispered. "You ruined... everything."

"I just balanced the ledger," I said.

The Shard let out a sound—a high, piercing shriek that bypassed my ears and vibrated my fillings. It was the sound of a hard drive head crashing into the platter.

The Ring—my father—was gone. Absorbed into the black.

I felt a hollow ache in my chest where the connection used to be. The weight was gone. The anchor was gone.

I lost him, the thought hit me, piercing the adrenaline. I saved the kid, I stopped the monster, but I lost him. Again.

There was no time to grieve. The Dead Pixel was expanding. It was no longer heart-sized. It was door-sized. And it was inhaling the room.

Scene 65

The noise rose to a frequency that erased thought.

A white blindness overtook the edges of my vision. The 'Buffer Underrun' was total. The universe had run out of memory to render the basement of the Cavaretta Estate.

I stood my ground, planting my feet on a floor that felt like slush.

I looked into the Shard.

It wasn't black anymore. It was... waiting.

The pressure in the room built. My ears popped. The air was sucked out of my lungs. The pressure was physical, heavier than the ocean, a crushing inevitability.

Every instinct in my primate brain screamed: Explosion. Shatter. Duck and cover.

I braced myself. I tensed every muscle. I prepared for the glass to break, for the blast wave to liquefy my organs, for the heat to strip the flesh from my bones.

The world held its breath.

The scream of the Shard climbed higher.
Higher.
Past sound.
Past pain.
Into silence.

I waited for the shatter.
I waited for the end.

But the glass didn't break.
The world didn't explode.

It just... opened.

Chapter 11

Chapter 11: The Shard & The Shatter

Scene 1

The crystalline shard doesn't shatter. That would imply physics, and we’re distinctly outside of that jurisdiction now. Instead, it undergoes a phase transition—from "object" to "event."

One moment, my palm is pressed against the smooth, vibration-dampened surface of the relic, the cold stone sucking the heat right out of my skin. The next, hairline fractures race down its vertical axis like lightning frozen in amber. The runic circle beneath my hand, seconds ago a dim auxiliary glow, blazes with a white-hot intensity that promises to cauterize my retinas if I don't blink.

I don't blink. I can't.

Then the sound hits.

It’s not a noise you hear with your ears. It’s a high-pitched whine that bypasses the auditory nerve entirely and drills directly into my molars, a frequency pitched so high it feels like my skull is being sanded from the inside out. A bass concussion follows—a rhythmic thrum that skips my heartbeat and rattles my occipital bone like a loose tooth in a jar.

Ozone. It’s the first thing to hit the senses, but calling it a "smell" is too polite. It’s an assault. The acrid, sterile, burnt-air scent of an electrical storm condensed into the single cubic meter of space I resolve in. It burns the lining of my sinuses, triggering a flash-memory of a blown transformer in the Ward 7-C basement—sparks showering onto wet concrete, the smell of fried insulation and impending darkness.

My mouth floods with a metallic copper tang. Capillaries in my nose have burst under the sudden pressure differential. I swallow blood and static.

Then the floor evaporates.

I fall. Not into the wine cellar floor, not onto damp earth, but into a cold, sucking void that offers no resistance, no friction, and no gravity. My knees buckle into nothingness.

The shard begins to scream.

It’s not a mechanical feedback loop or a magical hum. It’s a warbling, wet shriek that is unmistakably, horribly human. A chorus of throats tearing themselves apart in unison.

Unfiltered soul-data, my mind supplies. The thought comes with the terrifying, crystalline detachment of a man watching his own execution in high-definition. Compression algorithm failure. I just triggered an unhandled exception in sixteen centuries of Cavaretta math.

The cellar’s oak racks and damp stone walls begin to glitch. I watch them cascade into white pixels that stutter like corrupted video feed. The Soldatos flanking the circle—men with names, mortgages, favorite football teams—don't even get the dignity of dying. Their screams are truncated mid-syllable, clipped by a surgical edit as the physical world is garbage-collected around me.

I look up at the Victorian plaster scrollwork of the ceiling. It unspools like wet tissue paper in a monsoon, revealing a column of roaring white light where the parlor should be.

This isn’t destruction, I realize, watching the edges of my own vision begin to fray against the white expanse. This is a de-render. The Shard is treating reality as a memory leak.

Panic tries to claw its way up my throat, a biological imperative screaming that falling is bad and dissolving is worse. I shove it down. I’m a combat medic. Panic is a luxury for the triaged, not the triage officer.

Assess. Stabilize. Survive.

The mantra feels hollow here. There is nothing to stabilize. The environment itself is the wound.

My hand spasms, fingers curling into a fist around nothing. The ring on my finger—cold gold, heavy and scarred—burns against my knuckle. Not with heat, but with mass. In a world rapidly losing its definition, it feels impossibly solid. An anchor point in a dissolving sea.

Papa.

The presence in the metal is faint, static-filled, but it’s there. A stubborn refusal to be deleted.

I hold onto that sensation like a drowning man clutching a lifeline. Because right now, it’s the only thing convincing me I still exist.

Scene 2

At the epicenter of the white sprawl, Marco Cavaretta is still fighting.

Or trying to.

His arms are raised, his lips moving in a desperate, frantic counter-chant that should have been command-line authority. In the boardroom, or the ritual circle, that voice moved millions of dollars and bent reality. Here, it’s just noise.

Golden threads of mana spiral from his chest—filaments of pure capital, the accumulated "interest" of his lineage. But they aren't forming a shield. They’re being pulled inward. Sucked into the breach by a gravity that ignores the physics of wealth.

"NO!"

Marco’s voice cracks—a raw, wet scream that seems to lag behind the motion of his jaw, like a dubbed movie playing out of sync. "I am the investment!"

The words distort, pitch-shifting downward into an ugly, subsonic growl as time itself begins to stutter around him.

I watch, paralyzed by the sheer, chaotic wrongness of it. Marco’s fingers elongate, stretching like pulled taffy in a hot room. They unspool into golden filaments that whip toward the singularity’s core.

His face follows.

I see the skin pull away from the bone like wet clay in a high wind. His features—the arrogance, the fear, the Cavaretta jawline—lose their definition, smearing into streaks of light that are reabsorbed by the void.

The scream doesn’t fade. It’s simply clipped. Mid-vowel. As if a surgical hand somewhere in the backend code just deleted the audio track entirely.

He’s gone, I think. Just like that.

The stench hits me then—not ozone this time, but burnt sugar and rotting meat. The olfactory signature of soul-energy being metabolized. It flashes through the void, cloying and sickening, before vanishing as if the air recyclers just kicked in.

I bite my own tongue to keep from joining the chorus of screams that no longer exist. The pain is sharp, specific. The metallic taste of my own blood floods my mouth again. It’s the only thing anchoring me to my own body.

He’s being Garbage Collected.

The clinical phrase is a flimsy piece of sheet metal against the atrocity unfolding ten feet away, but I hide behind it anyway. The Shard doesn’t care about his lineage. It doesn’t care about his portfolio or his grand designs. It just sees a memory leak that needs to be plugged.

The other mages follow in a rhythmic, seismic cascade.

Thum-thum-thum.

A heartbeat amplified to a scale that rattles my teeth in their sockets.

One by one, the Soldatos and the minor mages twist, stretch, and are inhaled. I see a man I recognize—the one who held the door for us—disintegrate into a shower of golden motes. It’s almost beautiful, a kinetic sculpture of golden light and erasing history.

If you can ignore the fact that the medium is human lives.

Marco wanted godhood. He wanted to ascend. Instead, he’s become high-octane fuel for a machine he never understood.

Then the singularity turns.

There’s no face to look at, no eyes to meet, but I feel the shift in attention. It swivels toward me like a searchlight made of pure, needle-point need.

The heartbeat accelerates. Thum-thum-thum-thum.

Ozone floods back, sharp and electric. A tugging sensation begins at the center of my chest. It’s not physical—it’s not pulling on my skin or my clothes. It’s pulling on me.

Something is trying to extract my meaning from my meat.

Appetizers are done, I think, a hysterical bubble of laughter rising in my throat. Time for the main course.

Scene 3

Bianco Cavaretta isn’t screaming anymore. He’s past the point of vocalization, sliding across the frictionless white expanse of the manifold with his fingers clawing at a vacuum.

His shadow, the only dark thing in a world of blinding data, stretches toward the singularity’s core like pulled taffy. A physical representation of the existential weight trying to drag him into the recycle bin.

"BODHI!"

The name breaches the hush, a raw, hoarse shriek that cuts through the white noise of a collapsing reality.

I look at him. His eyes are fixed on mine, wide and glazed with the absolute terror of a man who just realized his invisibility has finally failed him. He’s the Sidekick. The comic relief. The guy who survives because he’s not important enough to kill.

But the Shard doesn’t know narrative tropes. It just eats.

I feel the tug at my own chest intensify. The Shard’s hunger focuses into a needle-point of demand, drilling into my sternum.

I should let him go.

The thought is cold, rational. Survival of the fittest. Triage protocol: save the salvageable. Bianco is dead weight. He’s a Cavaretta. He’s part of the rot.

But my body moves before my brain can sign off on the ethics. The combat-medic reflex—the hardwired biological imperative to fix, to save—fires hot and fast, overriding the logic.

Some Ol’ Bullshit, I think with a sudden, warped clarity. Protagonists make the sacrifice plays. It’s in the script.

I look at the singularity’s core. I don't see a god. I don't see a monster. I see a system. A spiral of golden metadata representing a compression algorithm.

And I see the lag.

There. A microsecond window between reclamation cycles. A stutter in the frame rate.

If I overclock my own conceptual mass, the calculation runs itself in the back of my mind, I can create a local exception. A priority object too heavy to process. For exactly one millisecond.

My hands blaze.

I don’t cast a spell. I open a wound in the fabric of the world.

The Blood-black Ouroboros manifests in a roar of anti-light, the serpent eating its own tail in a loop of creative destruction. It feels like shoving my arm into a blast furnace. The entropy screams through my channels, turning my veins into lines of molten lead.

I don’t aim at Bianco. I aim past him. Into the fold of space where the estate’s wards are flickering like a dying lightbulb.

The recoil is immense.

A concussive pop of displaced air rushes to fill the vacuum I just created. My shoulder dislocates with a wet, sickening crunch. The pain is dazzling, white-hot and immediate, grounding me in the moment.

The healing loop fires automatically, snapping the bone back into the socket before I can even scream. The grinding of cartilage sounds like rocks in a blender.

"EXIT!"

The word rips from my throat, raw and bloody.

The blast hits Bianco like a freight train of concentrated nothingness. It hurls him sideways, knocking him out of the singularity’s gravity well and through a chaotic fold in spacetime.

I watch him vanish into a burst of blue light—the ward boundary accepting him like a swimmer breaking the surface.

He’s out. Safe. The Sidekick has his reprieve.

The singularity roars.

It’s a frustrated, hungry sound that vibrates the atoms of my skeleton. The anchor I created by overclocking myself shatters instantly. The tugging at my chest becomes a violent pull.

I lose my footing—or what passes for footing in this void. I begin to slide toward the core, my feet scrabbling uselessly on a ground that is no longer there.

I taste blood. I taste triumph.

Narrative satisfied, I think, spitting a glob of red into the white. Sidekick saved. Now, the Protagonist faces the Boss.

My shoulder throbs in time with the singularity’s heartbeat.

Thum-thum-thum.

Bring it on, I think. And the void answers.

Scene 4

From my vantage point in the basement void—though "basement" is a memory and "void" is an understatement—I watch the world end.

The upper floors of the Cavaretta estate don't just fall; they fold.

It’s a slow-motion catastrophe. Victorian masonry, prize-winning privacy hedges, centuries of accrued architectural arrogance—all of it compresses into a spiraling column of debris racing toward the singularity’s maw. The estate isn't being destroyed. It’s being sucked into a vacuum that doesn’t recognize the concept of "up," "down," or "structural integrity."

A groaning, tearing cacophony fills the space between my ears. Wood splintering like dry bones. Stone cracking with the sound of gunshot. Glass shattering in a continuous, crystalline scream. The destruction is layered into a single, dissonant chord that vibrates in the marrow of my bones.

The air is thick with the scent of dying history. Ancient dust, older than the country itself. The sharp ozone of rain. The copper tang of exposed plumbing ripped from the walls. It creates an olfactory profile that I can only describe as house death.

Then gravity inverts.

My stomach lurches into my throat. I feel myself being pulled up—or what used to be up—toward the collapsing structure. It’s as if I’m standing on the ceiling of existence, watching the floor fall away into the sky.

Above me—or below, the distinction is meaningless now—the sky is visible through the gaps in the maelstrom. A swirling vortex of clouds is being sucked downward, dragging the stars with it. They flicker like dying embers in a storm, winking out one by one as they cross the event horizon.

This is what a god’s tantrum looks like, I think. The metatextual part of my brain is still running its commentary track, frantically trying to categorize the apocalypse into something manageable. The Shard is throwing a fit because someone poked it wrong. Some Ol’ Bullshit divine petulance.

I watch a gargoyle—one of the estate’s Gothic grotesques—tumble past me. As it crosses the threshold of the singularity, it stretches. Stone softens into putty. The face contorts, its silent snarl warping into something almost expressive—a mask of stone-cold terror—before it vanishes into the white.

The groaning of the building shifts. It deepens, smoothing out into a low, resonant hum. It sounds like a choir hitting a bass note that’s too low for the human ear to resolve, but perfect for the soul to tremble at. As if the singularity is finally singing a song it’s been composing for centuries.

A chandelier, still lit by some absurd miracle of electrical persistence, spirals past me. Crystal pendants catch the white light, scattering rainbows into the void before it hits the core and is gone.

And then, I am alone.

I am the last object with mass in a world of light.

Silence follows. It’s not a peaceful quiet. It’s a complete, absolute, aggressive silence. A weight against my eardrums. The air is gone, stripped away by the vacuum, leaving only the sterile nothingness of raw mana. Even the memory of scent has been deleted.

The singularity knows I have nowhere to go. It’s not rushing anymore. It pulses. Constant. Steady. Patient.

I am the last data point in the file. And the file is being closed.

Scene 5

Subjective time dissolves. There are no seconds, only frames.

My reality becomes a low-bandwidth video feed struggling to keep up with the data rate of a dying universe. Flickering images. Stuttering motion.

My body rotates without my consent. My feet rise above my head as "up" becomes "in." The singularity’s core is no longer a point in the distance; it’s an aperture. A massive, dilated pupil pulsing with a sickly golden light that promises the end of individuality.

I’m drifting toward it like a satellite in a decaying orbit.

A sound fills my skull—a digital rising tone, pure and piercing. It reminds me of the old dial-up modems, the sound of connection being established. The sound of a loading bar inching toward 99%. It vibrates my teeth. It vibrates my thoughts.

Then the pixelation begins.

It starts at my fingertips. I feel a tingling numbness, like circulation being cut off, but sharper. Cleaner. I look at my hand.

My fingers are no longer flesh. They are data.

Rectangles of colored light stutter where my knuckles should be. They flicker, rearranging themselves, converting from biology into a more efficient format.

I am becoming a file attachment, I think. The metaphor is absurd, a desperate joke cracked by a mind trying to armor itself against the terror of its own disappearance. High-bandwidth transfer. Some Ol’ Bullshit upload.

The pixelation spreads. My hands fade to translucent. I can see my veins—not as vessels carrying blood, but as lines of glowing code beneath thinning skin.

I’m not dying. Dying is messy. Dying is rot and decay. This is formatting. Cold data is replacing warm blood. Information is overwriting the messy redundancies of my biology.

And it hurts. Not like a wound, but like an amputation of the self.

The ring on my finger burns.

It’s a sudden, violent flare of presence against the numbness. My father’s transfigured form, the gemstone I’ve carried through fire and blood, is fighting the dissolution.

It turns hot. Then cold. Then hot again. It pulses against my pixelating finger, an anchor dragging in the mud, refusing to let the ship drift.

Dad’s still in the ring, I realize. I clutch at the phantom sensation with a hand that is increasingly made of light. He’s the only thing keeping me from being completely rewritten. The only thing with enough weight to survive the transition.

My vision blurs. Then it sharpens—but into something other.

I’m seeing the metadata of reality. The underlying code that defines shape and mass and time. The smooth white of the void resolves into streams of logic, equations describing the absence of matter. The sustained tone in my head fractures into harmonics—a chord of frequencies that describes the actual meaning of emptiness.

For a moment, I taste something.

Not blood. Not static.

Burnt sandalwood.

My mother’s favorite incense. Sharp, woody, comforting. It shouldn't be here. There is no air to carry it. But it floods my senses, a memory bleeding through the firewall of my mind.

Then the transition hits its peak.

The world collapses into a tunnel of rushing information. Bodhi Rao—the doctor, the healer, the man who said "Fuck Magic"—dissolves.

I am no longer a solid object. I am a stream of light hurtling into the dark.

Upload complete. Welcome to the Manifold.

DATA COMPRESSION ERROR.
RATIO: INFINITE.
ARCHIVE CORRUPTED.

Scene 6

Grey.

A limitless, suffocating expanse of grey.

Not darkness. Darkness has depth. This is flat. This is static. The visual snow of a detuned television, amplified to cosmic proportions and projected onto the inside of the universe’s skull.

I float in the center of it. Weightless. Formless.

I am acutely aware of the absence of my own biology. I don't breathe, because there is no air. I don't blink, because I have no eyelids. I simply perceive.

The sound is omnidirectional. A hissing roar of white noise that seems to originate from nowhere and everywhere at once. It’s not the aggressive silence of the void. This is the sound of a system waiting for input. The sound of a Loading Screen.

Great, I think, though I’m not sure what I’m thinking with. I’m stuck in the lobby.

I look down—or what my mind insists is "down."

A single point of solid color burns in the grey.

The ring.

It’s still there. Circling a phantom finger I can no longer feel, glowing with a soft, pulsing amber light. The white noise seems to recede around the metal, as if the ring creates a bubble of coherence in an ocean of noise.

Sandalwood.

I can smell it again, stronger now. It’s not just incense. It’s his aftershave. A sharp, clean scent that used to cling to his flannel shirts. It shouldn’t have survived the upload. Impossible.

But the impossible is the only thing that works here. It’s a metadata override. A user-defined variable that refuses to be cleared.

Dad, I think.

And for the first time in what feels like centuries, I feel a tear.

It’s real. I can taste the salt. I can feel the wet track on a cheek that shouldn't exist.

You’re still holding on. You’re still the anchor.

The static begins to thin.

Vague, ominous shapes coalesce in the distance. Monoliths of shadow standing patient and silent in the grey. The white noise resolves into whispers. A thousand voices, too quiet to parse, muttering just at the edge of perception. Each one a thread of biography being pulled from the Manifold’s archives.

A new scent floods the vacuum, overpowering the sandalwood.

Old flowers. Roses and lilies. The cloying, heavy sweetness of a funeral arrangement left too long in a warm room.

Reality is buffering. The next scene is loading.

A pressure builds in the space where my chest used to be. Not physical pressure, but existential. The sense that something vast—something ancient and hungry—is finally noticing me.

The Loading Screen is over. The Main Program is starting.

And I am not ready.

Scene 7

The Main Program doesn't load. It invades.

No signal. Anywhere.

That’s the first thing I know, because "seeing" is gone—not darkness, not black, not even the mercy of a void I can imagine. Just the absence of visual data. It’s like someone yanked the optic cable out of my brain and left the port open to the wind.

The grey static from the Loading Screen clears, and I’m blind.

And then the pressure arrives.

It’s not on my skin. It’s not on my lungs. It’s on me.

A low, throbbing hum replaces the whispers, bass so deep it vibrates phantom bones I’m not sure I still own. It’s the kind of frequency you feel in your teeth at concerts when the speakers are too big for the room.

Except the room is existence. And the speakers are screaming.

The smell of old flowers intensifies—roses, lilies, chrysanthemums. The heavy, cloying sweetness of viewing rooms and open caskets. It suffocates me, a blanket of perfumed death trying to cover up the truth: something rotting is nearby.

Salt stays on my tongue. Grief made tangible.

Weight stacks on top of weight.

At first it’s subtle, like the moment after you step into a crowd and you can tell the air is thick with other people’s moods. Then it multiplies. It crushes from all sides—psychic pressure, emotional gravity, the accumulated grief of a thousand souls pressing against the inside of my skull.

The shard’s battery is aware.

And it’s reaching for me.

My hands—do I have hands?—go cold with instinct. My left heel exists. My right doesn't. My spine is a question mark drawn in trembling chalk.

I’ve been here before.

Not in cosmic static, sure, but in Ward 7-C, when your body is detoxing and your brain is a broken radio picking up stations from hell. The only way you survive the flood is by building a wall between "you" and "everything."

Nurse Padma, I think, grabbing at the memory like a jagged rock. Four count. In-two-three-four. Hold-two-three-four.

Mental Firewall.

Dissociation as a tool, not a curse. A survival separation.

I close my nonexistent eyes. I build the firewall anyway.

First layer: Breath.
Except there is no breath, so I simulate it. I force the concept of lungs to expand against the pressure. In. Hold. Out. Hold. The rhythm gives my mind rails. A track to run on so it doesn't derail into the dark.

Second layer: Body.
Except there is no body, so I draw one in my head. Skeleton. Muscle. Skin. I map the boundaries. Here is where I end. Here is where the world begins. I trace the lines of my own silhouette in mental neon.

Third layer: Name.
Bodhi Rao.
The words are a pin hammered into the concept of self. I am Bodhi Rao. I am a combat medic. I am the son of Dinesh Rao. Use the details to staple the ego to the soul.

For one heartbeat, it works.

The hum fractures into individual frequencies. Not words—impressions. Personalities.

Fear that tastes like iron.
Rage that smells like diesel.
Resignation that feels like a heavy wool blanket pulled up to your chin.

Hospital antiseptic bleeds into the funeral flowers. The scent of death mixing with the desperate, sterile attempt to prevent it.

Metal fills my mouth. I realize I’m biting my phantom lip again. Hard.

Good. Pain is still an address. Pain means I'm home.

The ring warms under my grip.
Papa.
Presence against the flood.

Then the firewall buckles.

Not gradually. Not politely.
It buckles like a dam in an earthquake. A catastrophic, structural failure of the self.

A thousand lifetimes slam into me at once.

Within the Shard, the first thing that arrives is noise pretending to be memory—raw packets of terror slamming into my psyche faster than thought can label them.

But one packet is louder than the rest. One memory isn't noise. It's the Missing Frame.

The whiteout un-pauses.

Bang. Bang. Bang.

"Asset Retrieval," the voice booms. Not a Soldato. An Orderly. The bored, terrifying indifference of the incinerator crew.

"Protocol mandates immediate processing."

In the memory, I look at Papa. I look at the Ring. The distance between them is zero, but the distance between "Life" and "Object" is infinite. And I am about to bridge it.

"DISMANTLE."

I don't target the room. I target the probability. I grab the soul—the math, the essence, the him—and I rip it out of the biology.

I watch the violation happen. I watch flesh collapse into geometry. I watch the warm, messy, beautiful chaos of a human life get compressed, crushed, and formatted into the cold lattice of obsidian.

Flesh -> Math -> Stone.

The universe shrieks. A wet, tearing sound of a fundamental law being broken. Samsara weeping as I force a square peg into a round hole.

__pain.__

No one, Maa...Papa-- no one told me! No one helped me!

The words aren’t heard so much as injected. A child’s panic wearing my tongue. I taste the salt of tears that aren't mine. I feel the wrenching sob in a throat I don't possess.

Didn't tell me! Didn't Help me at all!

No one!

My Heart...

The pressure squeezes inward—an existential hand around my throat. I try to find the firewall again and come up holding only static.

Factory smoke. 1887. Metal shavings in my lungs. The scream of a steam whistle that means someone just lost a hand.
Not mine, I scream inwardly. Not my lungs!

I can **do** this!

Keep....keep it together...pull yourself back together.

I reach for breath and grab emptiness. I reach for body and find… gaps. My proprioception flickers. I have six fingers. I have no fingers. I have a thousand hands and they are all grasping.

I can pull this tog-- _pain . it hurts_...

Arms aren't--  

...my hands are missing...

My awareness flails where my limbs should be. The shard doesn’t care. It keeps streaming.

Sweat. Blood. 1901. A midwife's rough hands. The tearing pain of childbirth that splits the world in two. A joy so sharp it feels like terror.

I. Can **DO**. this.

MY HANDS!

I’ll kill you for this. My birthday-- I will kill you.

The threat isn’t mine. The fury fits anyway, like grief always does when it’s looking for a place to live. It slides into the grooves of my own anger, lubricating the mechanism.

Mama. Papa. Mama. Papa. Mama. Papa. Mama. Papa.

So cold. Hurting. **Monsters**!

.

My heart isn't beating.

The hum beneath reality fractures into personalities—impressions without words—until the impressions become a crowd. A mob in my mind.

Cordite. Mud. The sun rising over a trench. A cigarette burning between trembling fingers. A moment of peace before the whistle blows. Don't make me go over, the soldier thinks. Please don't make me go over.
I don't want to go over, I think.
We don't want to go over.

I hear so many **voices.**

The ring flares hot-cold against a finger that shouldn’t exist—Dinesh’s presence bracing like a shoulder under a door. The sandalwood scent spikes, sharp and woody, cutting through the rot of the flowers.
Hold on, the ring seems to say. Or maybe I'm saying it to myself. Or maybe we are saying it.

The name unleashes a fresh wave.
Not just Marco. His father. His grandfather.
You took my daughter.

The accusations layer over each other, a sediment of hate built up over centuries. And I feel the guilt. Not because I did it. But because I healed him. I fixed the monster's heart so it could keep beating.
I helped, I realized. We helped.
