AND WE
We…
…and…
And ...who...who exactly are we?
we...are...Bodhi? Bodhisunya?

The name feels strange in our mouth. Foreign. A sound someone else makes.

It's a ... strange name...for a child...isn't it?
We think my mama choose it for us...or was it papa...it must have been one of them or the other right?
We believe our papa had strong hands...big strong hands...do you know him?...do we know him...? Our papa...

We remember hands. Rough palms. Smelling of turmeric and surgical soap. Sunday mornings. The steam of coffee shifting the light in the kitchen.

He must have lifted us and carried us in those hands once
We can feel his hands...
Or did we carry him?
He seemed so small the last time we saw him
The last time we held him he was so small

The memory shifts. Darkens. The basement. The ritual. The smell of burning flesh that was also our flesh.

He could fit in our hands
He could fit on our finger

The realization hits us like a hammer to the oscillating bone.

The ring.
The stone.
The weight on our hand.

We think...we believe...we dream...?
He gave us this ring you know?
Our favorite ring.

Silence. A beat of pure, crystalline horror.

We wish they would come and see us sometime.
Our mama. Our papa.
They're both so far away.
Where did our parents go?
Why didn't they take us with them?

The guilt breaks the last firewall.
We turned him into a thing. We trapped him in the metal to save ourselves.
He is watching us dissolve. He is holding our hand while we disappear.

I killed him. The Ritual is a failure. I killed my father. And I turned him into a ring. And now people are going to die. Including me. I failed.

We Want...we want our mama...we want our mama we want our papa...
Please, we just want our mama and papa to come and get us and take us home. Please just let us go home.
We just want to go home. We want our mama.
Please mama.

Images burst across the blindness like lightning behind eyelids.
A child’s birthday party—1942, paper hats, ration-cake, a laugh that ends too abruptly when the door opens and the adults stop smiling.
A surgeon’s hands—1887, blood up to the wrists, knuckles raw from scrubbing, eyes that don’t blink because blinking wastes time.
A soldier’s final sunrise—trenches, cordite and mud, breath steaming, a cigarette held between trembling fingers like it’s a sacrament.

Each flash comes with a full-body memory.
Smoke.
Perfume.
Bread.
Blood.
Cordite.
Cough syrup.
Wet wool.
The sound of a train.
The taste of a final meal.
The taste of a kiss goodbye.
The taste of a tear swallowed in silence.

Everything.

Our "self" starts to fray at the edges. Not peeling, but dissolving. Like sugar in hot tea.
Bodhi becomes porous.
The Shard, without malice, merely tries to fit the deluge of memory in the only vessel available to it.
A container big enough to hold the archive.
A format that won’t reject the upload.

The flood rewrites the narrator.

Our thoughts splinter into broken shards of language—some ours, some borrowed, all sharp. Names repeat until they become a spell. Accusations surface like buoyant corpses. The Cavaretta curse fits our mouth too perfectly.

No sound. Only thought.
Only the pressure.
Only the sense of being eaten from the inside.

Our Mental Firewall tries to rebuild itself automatically, like scar tissue forming over and over in the same place.
It costs.
We feel something tear in the conceptual muscle behind our eyes.
A migraine without a skull.
A nausea without a stomach.
A panic without lungs.

The ring burns with presence, fighting to keep one thread of us intact.
Papa, we think, and it comes out as an anchor line thrown into a storm.
The warmth answers—faint, stubborn.
A grip.
But the flood is bigger than grip.

The firewall splits.
And the grief crashes through like a tidal wave.

We drown.
In lives.
In last breaths.
In the terrible, intimate physics of other people’s endings.

The final, clean boundary inside our mind—Bodhi Rao—warps under the weight.

We feel ourselves becoming plural.
Not metaphorically.
Literally.
We.

We are cold.
We are hurting.
We are missing our hands.
We are screaming for our mamas and Papas and gods that never answered.
We are furious.
We are afraid.

And somewhere deep inside the swarm, a small, bright thought flickers—mine, we think, mine—and it is so petty and human it almost makes us laugh.

Fuck.
Magic.

Then the shard leans in.
The weight increases.
More voices queue at the door.

And the last thing we feel…before any semblance of "I" becomes an outdated file format…is the ring pressing hot-cold against a finger that shouldn’t exist.
My father refusing to let the archive take everything without a fight.

The fight doesn’t feel heroic.
It feels like being held under.
And learning, too late, that we can drown in anything.

We reach out.
Not with a hand, but with a need.

"We are coming, mama…papa," we whisper, and the voice is a thousand voices.

The Manifold winks out.

Bodhi Rao is no longer a man.

Chapter 12

Chapter 12: Torrent of Souls

Scene 111

The crash is immediate and absolute. I don't fall into the Shard—I'm detonated by it.

A million images per second claw through my optic nerve with the violence of a physical assault. Each one is a full-body experience I have no consent to endure.

  1. A birthing room. I feel the mother's labor—the wrenching, rhythmic push that splits her pelvis. I am her and not-her, experiencing the wet, primal violence of delivery. The baby crowns. I scream with a throat that isn't mine, the taste of rust and sweat heavy on a tongue I can't control.

  2. An alley. The cold bite of a knife slides between ribs that occupy the same conceptual space as mine. The serrated edge catches on cartilage, a grinding vibration that travels up a spine I don't possess. I taste copper flooding a mouth that is suddenly filled with blood. My knees buckle, hitting wet pavement that smells of urine and old rain.

A kiss. Soft lips press against mine—or what used to be mine. The phantom taste of chapstick, cherry-flavored and waxy, from a mouth that turned to dust decades before I was born. I feel the flutter of a pulse against my fingertips, the specific, fragile rhythm of a heart in love.

A kaleidoscope of death scenes spins across my vision—faces contorting in the final, ugly honesty of agony. Wedding veils dissolve into funeral shrouds in a strobe-light flicker. The rhythmic flicker of hospital lights over gurneys never stops moving, a strobe effect that burns afterimages into retinas that are rapidly failing to process the input.

The cacophony of last words is an incoherent roar in my ears. A mother whispering a child's name overlaps with a soldier’s dying curse and a lover’s desperate, bargained plea. It’s a data-flood I was never meant to channel. Smell collides next: the iron tang of arterial spray, the smoke of cities burning, the cloying scent of cheap perfume masked by sweat, and the rot of the inevitable.

The mind-break racks my consciousness with relentless efficiency. I try to scream. My throat convulses, searching for vocal cords that don't exist. There's only the sensation of pressure building behind a face I can't feel. My sense of self erodes like sandstone in a hurricane. The version of me that once triaged alleyway casualties is now nothing more than a leaking container for a thousand endings. The trauma isn't something I watch; it's something I am. A sequence of recursive deaths webbing across my sense of self until the porcelain of my ego begins to crack.

Scene 112

Subjective time dissolves into a series of flickering snapshots, none of them my own.

I live a lifetime as a rice farmer. The ache in my lower back is rhythmic, metronomic, the percussion section of sixty years bent over emerald-green paddies. My hands—calloused, leathered, mapped with scars I don't remember getting—know the weight of rice stalks before my brain does. The monsoon drums overhead, a relentless white noise that seeps into my bones. Mud squelches between my toes, warm and alive and teeming. I know the taste of harvest-season rice, just-cooked, still steaming. I know the brittle, dry-mouthed grief of a failed harvest, years of labor condensed into a single, agonizing breath where the math of survival doesn't add up.

Then the channel flicks. Static bites at the edges of my perception.

I’m a corporate drone in a city that didn't exist when I was born. My fingers tap on ghost-keyboards, a staccato rhythm that matches the anxious flutter in a chest tight with caffeine and deadline stress. My eyes scan spreadsheets bathed in the sickly, buzzing light of office fluorescents—a frequency that drills a headache right behind my eyes. I understand the syntax of foreign thoughts now. Mandarin. Swahili. Urdu. They overlay my own inner monologue until my internal voice is a crowded room of shouting strangers.

The scents are a chaotic assault: the damp sweetness of wet earth and incense clashing with the industrial bite of toner ink and the cloying rot of fish in a sun-baked market. I taste stale coffee on a tongue that isn't mine, the warmth of mother's milk in a body that is once again small and feverish, and the iron of blood from a cheek bitten in a moment of panic I didn't author.

I live through the slow, agonizing decline of a dying child. The fever is a fire under my skin. I reach for a mother who never arrives, the small hand growing cold in the white void of the manifest.

My sense of 'Self' is butter spread over far too much bread, thinning until the boundaries of Bodhi Rao are nothing more than a suggestion. I forget New Canaan. I forget the hospice room. I forget the smell of antiseptic. I’m becoming an archipelago of borrowed selves, my singular point of awareness dissolving into a vast, collective ocean of trauma.

Scene 113

Amidst the screaming data-stream, a single file remains unparsed. Stubbornly corrupted. Protected.

'Papa.'

The name blinks in the conceptual white-out with the intensity of a lighthouse in a gale. It’s written in a font I recognize—my own handwriting, messy and hurried.

In that same heartbeat, the ring on my conceptual finger flares.

It burns with a tearing, physical heat. A branding iron of reality. It forces my identity back into a singular point, stapling the scattered pages of my soul back together. I gasp metaphysically—a soundless heave of lungs I no longer possess—and claw myself out of the collective hive mind with fingertips made of pure, raw desperation.

The chaos recedes. The roar of a thousand strangers’ lives is muffled by the sudden, rhythmic thrum of a single, distant heartbeat.

The scent hits me like a slap.

Old Spice.

Papa’s scent. Sharp. Spicy. Achingly familiar. It cuts through the cloying rot and the expensive perfumes of the Shard's other victims. It’s a sensory scalpel, slicing away the borrowed memories until only my own remain.

I remember the specific scuff on the shoes Papa wore to my wedding—the left toe, worn down from how he pivoted when he worked. I remember the hesitant, proud smile he gave me when I’d mastered my first complex healing sigil, the way the corners of his eyes crinkled.

This is the one grief that is his. That is mine. Not a shared file, not a collective trauma, but a personal, jagged wound that fits only the shape of my heart.

I cling to it like a life raft in a churning sea. The ring pulses against my spirit, a hot coal that anchors me to the name Bodhi Rao. I have one tether left in this void, and I will let it burn me to the bone before I let it go.

Scene 114

The medical part of my mind reboots with the cold, mechanical click of a system coming back online.

This is the part of me that fixes things. The part that dissected cadavers in freezing basements, seeing mechanics instead of humanity. The part that diagnosed mana-burn in back alleys by the smell of ozone alone. The part that performed forbidden Causal Inversions when the hospitals turned the poor away.

I look at the swirling storm of souls. I don't see tragedy anymore. I see data. I view it with the same clinical detachment I once reserved for terminally ill patients in the NCMA wards—assess, diagnose, treat.

The chaotic roar of lives coalesces into data I can interpret. I diagnose the phenomenon instantly: System Congestion. Unresolved Trauma Loops. Corrupted Exit Protocols. This isn't just a storm; it’s a malfunction on a sub-atomic scale.

I see the souls now as glitching wireframes. Fragmented holograms of trauma repeating on infinite loops. A child’s cry layered over a gunshot, which bleeds into the flatline of a monitor. The sounds don't just overlap; they stack, building a white noise of perpetual agony. They are polygonal tragedies, low-resolution renderings of high-definition pain.

In my mind, the air suddenly smells of sterile hospital corridors and industrial-grade disinfectant. It’s a grounding sensory memory, a tether to my training. I taste cold, clean water—a sharp contrast to the silt of foreign experience I’ve been swallowing.

The Shard isn't a battery, I realize. It’s a prison.

These souls aren't fuel—they're inmates, freshly processed and permanently stalled. I count them precisely. 1000 local nodes. Each one a human life trapped in its own final, broken moment.

The clinical clarity is my greatest weapon. It gives me the distance I need to find the one thing every prisoner dreams of.

An exit.

Scene 115

I scan the architecture of the void with the precision of a surveyor mapping a crime scene.

I run my conceptual hands along the white walls, looking for a seam, a crack, a back door. Anything that suggests the manifold has an edge.

There is none.

The white walls are infinite. A perfect geometric prison designed by alchemists who understood that the most effective way to trap a soul was to remove all points of reference.

I try to calculate trajectories for escape. My mind runs probability curves and conceptual thresholds in a rapid-fire sequence. I visualize vectors of force, angles of incidence. Each simulation collapses. Every path I project ends in a recursive loop that leads me right back to the center of my own awareness. Arrows of intent shatter against the curvature of this space.

The hum of the machine is omnipresent now. A low-frequency thrum that vibrates through my conceptual bones and rattles teeth I no longer have.

The absence of scent is itself a sensory assault. It's a sterile void that mocks my attempt to ground myself. I taste the bitter, metallic aftertaste of failure as one calculation after another hits a dead end.

Then, the realization strikes me with the force of a physical blow.

I'm too Heavy.

My soul-mass, weighted down by the density of my attachments, my memories, and the sheer gravity of my own identity, is what pins me to this white silence. To ascend, to escape the Shard's gravitational pull, I have to become weightless.

The obsidian ring on my finger feels like a leaden anvil. A precious weight that is keeping me from the horizon.

The calculus is brutal: the more I am, the less I can leave.

Scene 116

I look at the memory.

It's not a static image. It's a living, breathing moment stored in the core of my sequence—the root directory, the file that defines home.

The kitchen in Queens. Our kitchen. Cramped, barely eight feet by ten, with linoleum tiles that had seen better decades. One of them, third from the stove, had a crack shaped like a lightning bolt. I used to trace it with my toe during breakfast.

The morning light comes through the window at a forty-five-degree angle, catching the steam rising from two chipped ceramic mugs on the counter. The mugs are mismatched—one pale blue with a faded rooster, one cream-colored with a chip on the rim where I'd dropped it as a kid. The porcelain is warm in my small hands, almost too hot to hold, but I don't let go.

Papa is standing by the stove. He stayed home from the shop that day. Just to be with me. The memory of why is corrupted, lost to time, but the fact of his presence is diamond-hard.

He's humming. An off-key raga I don't know the name of—something his mother used to sing, maybe, or something he made up on the spot. The melody wobbles, missing notes, but it sounds like safety.

The kettle whistles. Not a scream, but a rising, insistent tone—E-flat, if I had to guess. The sound of water reaching the edge of transformation.

Papa lifts the kettle with one hand, pours the boiling water into the mugs with the ease of a man who's done this ten thousand times. The steam rises in twin columns, dancing in the morning light like miniature stars.

Then the clink. The sharp, metallic percussion of a spoon mixing sugar. He stirs three times clockwise, then taps the rim twice. A ritual. His ritual.

The scent arrives last, the way scent always does in memory—complex, layered, overwhelming.

Cardamom, sharp and almost astringent, the green pods cracked open in the pot.

Ginger, earthy and warm, the kind that burns the back of your throat if you breathe it in too fast.

Boiled milk, sweet and comforting, coating the spices with softness.

And underneath it all, cutting through everything—Old Spice. Papa's aftershave. The smell of his collar when he hugged me. The smell of him.

This is the seed. The core file. The definition of what I used to call love.

And to escape the Shard, I don't just have to leave this memory.

I have to un-feel it.

I have to sever the emotional pointer that makes this moment precious. I have to take Papa—this man standing at the stove, humming his off-key song, pouring chai with calloused hands—and turn him into just another stranger. Another ghost in the static.

The ring on my finger feels like a leaden anvil.

I feel the weight of Papa's hand on my shoulder—phantom pressure, the memory of safety. It's keeping me here. Pinning me to this void with the gravity of love.

The calculus is brutal: I can keep this warmth and drown, or I can cut it out and ascend.

I look at Papa's back. The steam rising from the chai. The morning light making the dust dance.

And I raise the scalpel.

Scene 117

I act.

There's no hesitation left. Only the cold, hard certainty of a man who's already calculated the loss and decided the math is worth it.

I visualize the Ego—not as the self, but as a tumor. Benign, perhaps. Precious, maybe. But nonetheless killing the patient.

I raise the blade of my will. It's been honed by a hundred desperate procedures in a hundred dark alleys. By every time I chose to cut instead of comfort, to excise instead of preserve.

And I cut.

The words don't need to be spoken aloud. They echo in the void anyway, ratified by something older than magic, colder than physics.

I trade my ability to feel this love for the physics to escape it.

It's a Binding Vow. Absolute. Irreversible. The universe hears it and says: Accepted.

I watch as the memory of my father begins to desaturate.

It starts at the edges. The kitchen walls lose their cream color first, fading to a dull beige, then to gray. The cracked tile turns monochrome. The linoleum loses its mustard-yellow tint.

Then the steam. The dancing motes of light flatten into grayscale fog.

The chai itself—the deep amber of the liquid—drains away like blood from a wound, leaving only a flat, textureless gray.

Papa's face is next.

The warmth leeches out frame by frame. The brown of his skin becomes a neutral gray. The depth in his eyes—the way they crinkled when he smiled—flattens into a geometric arrangement of pixels.

The smile that used to mean safety becomes just a configuration of facial muscles. Data points. Coordinates on a face that could belong to anyone.

The laugh—the off-key humming that used to make me feel like the world had an order—flattens into a digital audio sample. Stripped of resonance. Just sound waves playing in a vacuum.

The scent of Old Spice vanishes.

One moment it's there, sharp and familiar, cutting through the rot and chaos of the Shard.

The next, there's nothing. Not even the memory of the scent. Just the sterile void of the manifold.

I taste numbness spreading across my tongue. A void where the salt of tears used to live.

I feel a sudden, terrifying lightness in my chest. As if my heart has evaporated and left a vacuum in the center of my ribcage.

The warmth is gone.

The ache is gone.

I remember that my father exists. I remember his name. I remember the fact of loving him.

But I can no longer feel it.

I am hollow.

Scene 118

The weight is gone.

The instant I sever the emotional pointer, the universe seems to exhale. The gravitational pull of my identity, the dense soul-mass that had anchored me to this prison, dissolves like sugar in hot water.

I'm suddenly 'Aerodynamic' to the flow of the void. The souls that had been tearing at my ego moments ago now slide off me like water off a duck’s back; there are no hooks left for them to catch on.

I'm no longer a container for their trauma; I'm a conduit. A void through which they pass without friction.

I'm no longer human. I'm a Void-Construct—a hollow architecture of a man, functioning without the firmware of feeling.

I float in the white silence.

For the first time since the ritual began, it is truly quiet. The roar of a thousand lifetimes has faded to a whisper, and the whisper has faded to nothing.

I exist. I perceive. But I don't feel.

It’s the absolute zero of the soul. Cold. Perfect. Empty.

The survival mechanism has worked. I have paid for my life with my humanity, and the receipt is printed on the blank slate of my mind.

Scene 119

The void responds to my emptiness. It recognizes me now—not as a prisoner, but as an operator.

There is no mystical opening of a third eye. No gruesome tearing of skin on my forehead. The change is intrinsic, a rewrite of my optical firmware.

My eyes burn.

The sensation is torrential, as if someone is pouring liquid nitrogen into my sockets. The sclera flood with ink-black mana, obscuring the white in a rising tide of void. My irises fracture, spinning, reconfiguring into complex golden mandalas that act as Heads-Up Displays for reality itself.

I see the 'Source Code' of the manifold scrolling in red glyphs across my vision. It’s a language I have never learned but suddenly understand perfectly. Sans-serif. Brutalist. The raw syntax of creation.

I see the Exit protocol. I see the universe rendered as raw data.

The 1000 souls aren't screaming anymore; they are processes, background tasks waiting for a task manager to allocate resources.

I understand now. Magic isn't a miracle bestowed by gods. It isn't a gift. It's an operating system, and I've just cracked the root password.

The smell of ozone and petrichor fills my new senses—the scent of reality being rewritten at the command line. I taste metal and blood, the flavor of my own biology being forcibly upgraded to run high-performance software.

My head throbs, not with pain, but with expansion. My skull feels like it’s stretching, cracking, groaning under the pressure of the data flooding into it.

I'm no longer a prisoner.

I'm the Admin.

Scene 120

I reach out with my new perception. Not with hands, but with intent.

The Eye of Ajna dissects the manifold’s architecture in microseconds. It highlights boundaries, exit nodes, and causal links in shimmering gold overlays.

I don't search for the exit. I declare it.

I grab the fabric of the infinite white void with conceptual hands that now wield admin-level authority. I don't ask to leave. I don't pray for release. I command the scene change.

'End Instance.'

The voice isn't my own. It’s a chorus—the unified will of the 1000 souls I've processed. Their trauma transmuted into raw power. Their screams harmonized into a single, divine imperative.

The command echoes through the void with the force of a thunderclap in a cathedral.

The white walls shudder. Black cracks web across the infinite expanse like veins of obsidian lightning, splintering the perfection of the prison. The fractal pattern spreads, jagged and beautiful.

The manifold, which has held me prisoner for subjective eons, obeys the new logic. It shatters like a dropped mirror. Thousands of shards rain down into nothingness.

I fall through the cracks. Not down, but out.

The sensation is one of violent acceleration, a rush of motion that feels like being launched from a cannon directly into the heart of the physical world. Gravity grabs me by the throat and pulls.

Scene 121

I hit the wet earth hard.

Gravity returns with a brutal, welcoming slap. It knocks the wind out of me, compressing lungs that suddenly need air again.

I lie there for a moment, tasting mud and ash. The sensation of physical weight settles back into my bones, heavy and undeniable.

I'm in the crater—the smoking, ruinous aftermath of the Cavaretta Estate. It’s just a scar in the earth now, under a weeping gray sky. Rain hisses against the scorched ground, washing away the evidence of the ritual in rivulets of gray sludge.

I stand up slowly.

My limbs feel foreign. Lighter. Hollow. As if my bones are now made of bird-glass, fragile and resonant.

I feel… less. The ache of Papa’s loss is still there, a factual entry in my memory, but it is distant now. A file in a closed folder. Not an open wound bleeding meaning into my chest. I poke at the memory and feel only the smooth scar tissue of the Vow.

I walk to a puddle formed in the depression of a blast mark and look down.

My reflection stares back from the dark water.

The sclera are void-black. The irises are intricate gold rings, spinning with the faint, rhythmic pulse of processing data. The Eye of Ajna, freshly opened and fully calibrated, pulses faintly on my brow beneath the skin.

I flip the obsidian ring on my finger—the specimen of my sacrifice. The band is cool against my skin, indifferent to the price I paid for it. It is no longer a memento. It is evidence. A relic of the man I used to be.

I'm back. I'm awake. And I'm something else entirely.

Chapter 13

Chapter 13

Scene 1

The world did not end with a bang, but with a hollow sound. A sound like a god inhaling the sky, followed by the wet, percussive slap of reality trying to fill the vacuum.

Bianco Cavaretta lay face down in the slurry of what used to be the family estate's rose garden. It was a place he knew better than his own reflection. He knew the specific ph of the soil here (acidic, tweaked for the hybrid tea roses). He knew the layout of the trellis work (imported ironwood from the Northern Zone). He knew that if he turned his head three degrees to the left, he would see the marble fountain his grandfather had commissioned to celebrate the family’s ascent to the High Council.

Now, there was only ash.

The mud was boiling. It pushed against his cheek, hot and viscous, smelling of ozone, burnt sugar, and the coppery, unmistakable tang of atomized blood. It wasn't just mud; it was a soup of destruction, a suspension of everything Bianco had ever called home, ground down to its molecular substrate.

He tried to push himself up. His brain sent the command—elevation, urgency, flee—but the signal died somewhere in his spinal cord. His arms were shaking so violently they wouldn't lock. He managed to roll onto his side, the movement agonizing, like grinding broken glass into his hip. He coughed, a wet, rattling sound, and spat out a mouthful of black phlegm that tasted of propellant and death.

His bespoke silk suit—a gift for his twenty-first birthday, tailored by the finest weaver in Rome, a garment that cost more than a citizen earned in a decade—was fused to his skin in places. He could feel the ruined fabric pulling at his burns, a constant, stinging reminder of his own fragility.

Nonno.

The memory hit him harder than the blast wave. It wasn't a vague sense of loss; it was a high-definition assault. He remembered the smell of his father's cologne—sandalwood and expensive tobacco—that had lingered in the library just this morning. He remembered the weight of his father’s hand on his shoulder during the Solstice Gala, the pride in the old man's voice.

“Resistance is legacy, Bianco. We do not break. We endure.”

And then, the light. The blinding, white-hot silhouette of his uncle standing against the sun, a figure of pure radiant energy. And Papa standing there, defiant, unbreaking.

And then... simply ceasing to be.

Not dying. Dying implied a process. Dying implied a body, a funeral, a transition. This was deletion. One moment, the patriarch of House Cavaretta was a pillar of the world; the next, he was physics. He was heat. He was a shadow burned into the retina of the universe.

Bianco wept. It was a small, pathetic sound in the silence of the crater, a whimper that had no place in the aftermath of such divine violence. He wiped his eyes with a trembling hand, smearing soot and blood across his face, effectively blinding himself with the grime of his own survival.

He blinked, trying to clear the grit. He looked toward the epicenter.

He expected to see a corpse. He needed to see a corpse. He needed to see the charred, twisted remains of the cousin he had tried to save, the cousin whose instability had birthed this nightmare. He needed Bodhi to be dead. If Bodhi was dead, then this was a tragedy. A terrible, incomprehensible accident.

But if Bodhi was alive... then this was a war crime. This was a murder.

The smoke cleared, stripped away by the heavy, radioactive rain that had begun to fall.

And Bianco saw the Monster.

It stood in the exact center of the glassed earth, at the focal point of the deletion event. It was naked, its skin pale and luminous against the charcoal ground, like a shard of moonstone dropped into a coal bin. It wasn't burned. It wasn't bleeding. It was pristine.

But it wasn't standing still.

It was... vibrating.

Bianco squeezed his eyes shut and opened them again, certain he was suffering from a concussion. But the image didn't resolve. The figure—Bodhi—seemed to be skipping frames in reality's render cycle. One second he was facing north, looking at the ruin of the manor house; the next, he was facing south, staring into the dark tree line. There was no transition. No blur of motion. Just Variable A becoming Variable B without passing through the space in between.

His limbs moved with a fluid, aquatic grace that defied the physics of gravity. He didn't shift his weight; he simply altered his position relative to the ground.

And the noise.

It wasn't a hum. It wasn't the wind. It was the sound of a hard drive scratching, amplified to a deafening screech that bypassed the ears and vibrated directly in the jawbone. It was the sound of logic failing. It was the high-pitched whine of reality complaining that something existed within it that was violating its Terms of Service.

The rain fell harder, a torrential downpour of cold, acidic water.

But it didn't touch him.

Bianco watched in horror as the raindrops curved around Bodhi’s form. They didn't bounce off a shield; they simply refused to occupy the same coordinates. The space around Bodhi was a dry zone, a bubble of rejected causality. The water feared him. The air seemed to recoil from his skin, creating a shimmering heat haze that distorted the background.

"Bodhi?" Bianco whispered. The name felt like a curse on his tongue. It felt like summoning a demon.

The figure stopped vibrating. The transition was instant. From 100 mph blurring to absolute statuesque stillness in zero seconds. Inertia was ignored.

The head snapped toward him—too fast. Necks shouldn't move that fast. Vertebrae should snap. Muscles should tear.

But the Monster just smiled.

The eyes were gone. There were no whites, no pupils, no irises. There were just twin pools of spinning gold fractals, infinite geometric loops eating the light around them. They were windows into a furnace, into a place where math burned hot enough to melt souls.

Bianco stopped weeping. He forgot how to breathe. He forgot his father. He forgot the estate.

In the face of this anomaly, grief was a luxury he could no longer afford. There was only the primal, lizard-brain terror of a prey animal realizing it has locked eyes with the apex predator of a new ecosystem.

The Monster took a step. The mud didn't squelch. It didn't yield. The ground seemed to harden to meet his foot, eager to support him.

Bianco tried to crawl backward, digging his heels into the sludge, but his body was failing him. The terror was a paralyzing agent.

Run, his mind screamed. Run before it deletes you too.

But he couldn't move. He could only watch as the thing that wore his cousin's face began to drift toward him, carried by a wind that didn't exist.

Scene 2

The Monster smiled.

It wasn't a human smile. It was too wide, showing too many teeth. It was a rictus of pure, chemical mania. It looked like the expression of a man who had stared into the heart of a nuclear explosion and found it funny.

"Little Cavaretta," the Thing said.

The voice did not come from its mouth. It came from the air, from the mud, from the buzzing inside Bianco's molars. It sounded like a choir of thousands, all screaming in digital harmony.

"Little white stallion," the voice distorted, pitching down into a bass that rattled Bianco's ribs. "Did you lose your saddle?"

Bianco scrambled backward, his heels digging into the sludge. "Stay back," he whimpered. "Stay back, you... you thing."

Bodhi didn't walk. He just... occurred closer.

A flicker of black static, and suddenly he was standing five feet away. Another flicker. Two feet.

Bianco could see the details now, and he wished he couldn't. Bodhi's skin was translucent, revealing veins that pulsed with black light instead of blood. There were symbols—glowing, golden glyphs—crawling under his epidermis like worms.

Scene 2

The Monster smiled.

It wasn't a human smile. It was a geometry problem. It was too wide, stretching the skin past the point of elasticity, revealing too many teeth that looked too white, too sharp, too... new. It was a rictus of pure, chemical mania, the expression of a man who had stared into the heart of a nuclear explosion and found the math hilarious.

"Little Cavaretta," the Thing said.

The voice did not come from its mouth. Bianco saw the lips move, but the sound didn't sync. It came from the air, from the mud, from the buzzing inside Bianco's molars. It sounded like a choir of thousands, all screaming in digital harmony, layered over a bass track that felt like an earthquake.

"Little white stallion," the voice distorted, pitching down into a sub-frequency that rattled Bianco's ribs like a cage. "Did you lose your saddle?"

Bianco scrambled backward, his heels digging furrows into the radioactive sludge. "Stay back," he whimpered, his voice cracking like dry wood. "Stay back, you... you thing."

Bodhi didn't walk.

Walking involved locomotion. Walking involved leverage, friction, the transfer of kinetic energy. Bodhi simply... occurred closer.

A flicker of black static—like a corrupted video file—and suddenly he was standing twenty feet away. Another flicker. Ten feet. There was no movement, only a re-writing of coordinates. The air displaced by his sudden arrival cracked like a whip.

Cousin Bodhi. The failure. The Null. The boy who couldn't light a candle with magic if his life depended on it. Bianco remembered him tripping over his own robes at the academy. He remembered him stuttering through basic theory exams. Make-fun-of-him Bodhi. Pity-him Bodhi.

That boy was gone. Deleted.

Bianco could see the details now, and he wished he couldn't. Bodhi's skin was translucent, glowing with an inner, sickly light. Beneath the epidermis, veins pulsed not with red blood, but with black, viscosity-free light. And deeper still, etched into the very muscle fibers, were symbols—glowing, golden glyphs that crawled and shifted like living worms.

"Monster," Bianco breathed. It was the only categorization his brain could offer.

Bodhi tilted his head. The gold fractals in his eyes spun faster, a kaleidoscope of impossible geometry.

"Monster?" the voice echoed, layering over itself—soprano, alto, bass, static. "That IS a categorization error, cousin. Check the scoreboard. We are the only reason you aren't currently participating in the water cycle as a fine red mist."

Use of the plural. We.

Bianco felt his bladder loosen. The horror was total. "You killed him. You killed my father. You murdered him."

"We deleted an obstacle," Bodhi corrected, his tone bored, clinical. "He was holding onto legacy code. Deprecated software. He tried to upload a firewall when the system was already formatting the drive."

He reached out. His hand was wreathed in black sparks that hissed as they touched the oxygen, burning holes in the very air.

"And you know what happens to legacy code, Bianco?"

Bianco shut his eyes, waiting for the end. Waiting to be deleted. He tried to think of a prayer, but the Gods felt very far away, and the Monster was very, very close.

Scene 3

A finger touched his nose. Boop.

It didn't feel like flesh. It felt like touching a live wire, a localized polarity reversal that sent a jolt of static electricity snapping against Bianco's skin.

He yelped, his eyes flying open.

Bodhi was leaning in, mere inches away, that terrible, shark-like grin stretching his face to the breaking point. The gold eyes were spinning so fast they were a solid disk of light.

"Sike," Bodhi whispered.

The word hung in the air, heavy and wrong.

Then, the world snapped back. The static vanished. The choir of voices cut out, leaving only one: Bodhi’s familiar, mocking tenor.

Bodhi pulled back, executing a pirouette that looked like a video game character glitched into a loop, spinning three times before landing in a perfect crouch.

"I'm just fuckin' with ya, B!" Bodhi crowed, pointing a black-clawed finger at Bianco. "Look at your face! You look like a constipated gargoyle who just realized he’s made of stone!"

The laugh that followed was a wet, barking sound. It wasn't the laugh of a sane man. It was the laugh of someone whose sanity had snapped so cleanly it had left no jagged edges to snag on reality.

Bianco stared at him, his mind unable to bridge the gap. The cognitive dissonance was physically painful. One moment, he was facing an eldritch horror, a god of entropy. The next, his cousin was making a 'your mom' joke in the middle of a blast crater. The whiplash made him want to vomit.

"What... what are you?" Bianco whispered, his voice trembling.

Bodhi stopped laughing. He stood up, throwing his arms wide. Black lightning arced from his fingertips, scorching the mud, writing equations in the ash that faded before Bianco could read them.

"Survival!" Bodhi shouted to the storm. "We are the Update! We are the Patch! The regulators are coming, cousin! The devs are trying to roll back the server!"

Bianco heard it then. Beneath the rain, beneath the ringing in his ears.

The low, rhythmic thrumming in the distance. Engines. Heavy, military-grade turbines displacing air.

"NAMF," Bianco realized, the name tasting like cold iron. "The drones."

"The Fun Police!" Bodhi corrected, grabbing Bianco's arm. His grip was like iron pliers, unyielding and hot. "Time to go! Chop chop! I want to see if I can outrun a missile! I bet I can. I bet I can outrun the concept of 'impact'!"

He hauled Bianco to his feet as if he weighed nothing. Bianco stumbled, his legs jelly, his equilibrium shattered.

"Bodhi, we can't," Bianco sobbed, the hopelessness crashing down on him. "There's nowhere to go. They'll kill us. Look at this... look at what you did. There’s nothing left."

Bodhi turned. The gold eyes locked onto him, and for a second, the mania vanished. The laughter died. In its place was a cold, terrifying clarity. The clarity of a machine that had calculated the odds, run the simulation a billion times, and decided that the outcome was irrelevant because the game itself was rigged.

"They can try," Bodhi said softly. "But I think we're going to find that their permissions... have been revoked."

He turned and dragged Bianco toward the smoke of the shipping yards. Toward the killing fields.

And Bianco followed, because the monster in front of him was the only thing scarier than the monsters behind.

Scene 4

Scene 4

Input flood.

That’s the first thing. Not pain. Not fear. Just Data. A tsunami of raw, unfiltered information crashing against the firewall of my consciousness.

I’m not seeing the world anymore. I’m seeing the wireframe.

The mud isn't mud; it’s a colloidal suspension of silicon dioxide (SiO2), water (H2O), and decomposed organic particulate matter, currently vibrating at a thermal frequency of 45.2°C due to residual radiation.

The rain isn't rain; it’s a kinetic bombardment of acidic droplets, each one carrying a specific pH value (4.2) and a velocity vector (9.8 m/s²).

It hurts.

It’s like staring at the sun without eyelids. It’s like listening to every radio station on earth simultaneously. The "Eye of Ajna"—the new hardware I just forced into my spiritual socket—is running at 100% capacity, and I don't have the drivers to filter the feed. My brain is overheating. I can feel the synapses firing so fast they’re stripping the myelin off my neurons.

"Bodhi!"

A hand grabs my shoulder. Physical contact.
Source: Bianco Cavaretta.
Force: 45 Newtons.
Skin Temperature: 98.6°F.
Heart Rate: 142 BPM (Tachycardic stress response).
Cortisol Levels: Critical.

I blink, and for a second, the wireframe overlay glitches out, revealing Bianco's terrified face. He looks like a smudge of oil paint on a pristine digital canvas.

"We have to move," he screams, his voice cutting through the digital screeching in my head. "The garrison is active! Look!"

He points.

My eyes track the vector. I don't just look; I Ping.

Zoom: 4x. 8x. 16x.
Enhance: Thermal. Night Vision. Mana-Sensitivity.

Through the fog, I see them.

Not one drone. Twelve.

A full hunter-killer squadron. NAMF Sentry-Class v4.0. I read their specs off the manufacturer's plate welded to their chassis, visible even through the rain. General Dynamics / Thaumaturgic Division. Carbon-fiber chassis reinforced with woven mithril filaments. Levitational drive cores hum with a frequency of 440Hz—the note of A, perfectly tuned for murder.

Their weapon arms are spun up. Gatling-armatures loaded with 12.7mm depleted uranium rounds, tipped with hollow-point negation runes. Designed to punch through tank armor and magic shields alike.

They are sweeping the shipping yard in a grid pattern. Efficient. Deadly. A networked hive-mind of localized violence.

A normal person would see monsters. I see... math. I see a physics equation trying to balance itself to zero.

"Run," Bianco chokes out, pulling at my sleeve. He’s trying to drag me toward the cover of a rusted shipping container. "Bodhi, please!"

But I don't move. I can't.

My body feels heavy, vibrating with the residual energy of the resurrection. The entropy is pooling in my gut, hot and heavy like molten lead. My bones feel like they're vibrating at a different frequency than my flesh.

"Bodhi!" Bianco shrieks. "They'll see us!"

"They already have," I murmur.

My peripheral vision catches the red targeting lasers painting the fog. Twelve lines of coherent light, cutting through the steam, all converging on our position.

Target Lock initiated.
Threat Assessment: High.
Action: Terminate.

I can hear their internal comms. Not with my ears, but with my mind. They're shaking hands.

Unit 7 to Unit Leader: Target acquired. Biological matches fugitive profile.
Unit Leader to Squad: Engage. Free fire zone authorized.

I grin. My face feels like it’s cracking, wet clay drying in a kiln.

"Let 'em come, B," I whisper, the mania bubbling up again. "I need to calibrate the sensors. I need to test the new cooling system."

Scene 5

The first bullet hits me in the left shoulder.

It comes in low, mean, clean—the drone’s muzzle twitching, physics committing.

The world snaps into debug.

Time keeps its pace; my brain overclocks—threat-response as interface, fear as math.

Rain stipples my eyelashes and turns the yard into gray slurry. Ajna tags 1,142 droplets on my left sleeve alone.

The round leaves the drone with a clean, brutal cough. Gilding-metal jacket. Rifling signature stamped into it like a thumbprint. The air in front of the nose cone buckles into a faint shimmer, rain getting bullied out of the way. Ajna hands me the number like a label on a specimen jar:

2,800 feet per second.

I see my shoulder as a coordinate. Angle of entry. The yaw starting to hunt—just enough to tear instead of cut. Pressure builds in the air a split-second before impact, a soft, ugly whump under the drone’s cough. I watch the copper kiss skin and stop being a projectile and start being a problem.

Impact detonates inside my joint. Energy blooms through muscle and bone with the kind of certainty physics reserves for car crashes. My deltoid tears open. My clavicle powders. The round exits out the back and takes a jagged bite of scapula with it, carving a fist-sized window straight through me.

The force rewrites my posture. My torso lurches, shoulder dragged backward like a hinge ripped off a door.

Warm mist erupts into cold rain. It hangs there for half a beat—red, fine, indecent—before the wind smears it into the yard.

I smell iron instantly. Fresh. Bright. Too alive.

Bianco screams.

It’s raw enough to register under the gunfire, a sound that comes from the bottom of him. He sees me die. He hears the end of the only person in front of him who’s still moving.

He’s behind me somewhere—two steps, three—boots sucking at mud, breath catching, hands hovering like he can grab a miracle by the collar and yank it back.

My body stays quiet. The part of me that yelps and flinches gets shoved aside like a tab I don’t have time to look at.

Pain arrives anyway. It hits the back of my throat first, hot and metallic, then climbs into my skull like a nail being hammered in slow.

I look down.

Meat. Bone splinters. Nerve threads snapped white and fraying. Steam rising off exposed tissue because the rain can’t cool it fast enough.

The cavity through my shoulder flashes daylight and wet darkness. I can see my own ribs through the gap if I tilt just right.

That should make me gag.

My brain flags it as a fault.

Error Code

Critical Failure: Structural Integrity Lost at Sector 7 (Left Shoulder).
Blood Loss: 0.435 liters/second.
Integrity Timer: 2.3 seconds.
Write Access: GRANTED (SUBSTRATE).
Suggested Action: Restore.

I focus.

Ajna overlays keep stacking—vectors, stress maps, latency warnings. Every warning wants my attention. I give it a thin slice and keep the rest for the problem in front of me.

Clerics negotiate. They pray, they plead, they route their will through sanctioned channels and hope the gods feel generous. That’s the Academy’s whole theology: user permissions, approved endpoints, sacred rate limits.

I rewrite.

I reach into the wound with my mind—past the gore, past the shock—like sliding my fingers into a seam behind the wallpaper. It feels wrong, intimate in the way surgery is intimate. I grab the frayed nerve endings. The bone dust. The torn muscle fibers that still remember their original shape. I pull up the file history of my own body and check the timestamp.

0.482 seconds ago.

I loop it.

CTRL+Z.

Black and gold flares off my shoulder, and reality stutters. The frames misalign. For a fraction of a second my arm exists in overlapping versions—intact, ruined, intact—like the world can’t decide which render to commit to.

The yard doubles for a blink. The rain repeats in place, then catches up.

Then the correction lands.

Bone reseats with a wet hydraulic crack that echoes off shipping containers. Muscle braids itself together in tight, fractal geometry that ignores every medical textbook I ever skimmed. Tendons rethread with a neatness that makes the rest of biology look like a prank. Skin seals over.

No scar.

Just a faint Ouroboros shimmered into the grain of me—spinning once, hot as a star in its last second, then gone.

My shoulder rolls like nothing happened.

“Bodhi…” Bianco breathes.

He’s frozen. Mouth open. Eyes too wide. Watching catastrophe run backward and trying to find the missing frames.

His hands shake in the air, undecided between grabbing me and backing away.

“It’s just rain, B,” I say.

My voice comes out level. Steady. Wrong.

The pain is present—white-hot and absolute—driving straight into the base of my skull. I shove it somewhere quiet. I mute it. A screaming alert turned into a blinking icon I can ignore.

Underneath that, something else ticks: the cost meter, the invisible stomach-count. My mouth waters like I’m starving.

Advance

I start walking.

“Bodhi, no!” Bianco yells, boots slipping in slush and mud as he scrambles after me. “Get down! You’re insane! You’re going to get yourself erased!”

His voice breaks on erased.

I don’t duck. I don’t sprint for cover. I step into the open like the yard belongs to me.

The drones hover ahead in a loose line, sleek and humming, their weapon arms already spun up. Red optics, flat and patient. Twelve of them, evenly spaced, as if someone arranged death with a ruler.

Their optics track, then hitch.

They chirp—short, discordant stutters. Confirmation logic eating its own tail.

Target Damaged.
Target Intact.

Logic Error.

They open fire in unison.

The sound isn’t one gun. It’s a sheet of synchronized violence, like industrial nailers emptying into steel. Casings tumble and ping off metal somewhere. Mud kicks up in little brown eruptions around my feet.

Rounds slam into me hard enough to shove my whole body backward with each impact.

Thwack-thwack-thwack-thwack.

My stomach opens. Intestine snaps in three places like wet rope. Heat spills out, then cold rain rushes in.

Loop. Restore.

The tear vanishes mid-drip. Flesh reknits with a cruel calm. My gut lurches as everything returns to its seat.

A round punches through my right thigh and breaks the femur with a dry, ugly pop. For a microsecond I feel bone scrape bone.

Loop. Restore.

The leg straightens itself before my knee finishes buckling. The bruise never gets a chance to bloom.

A cluster catches my chest. My left lung collapses with a flat, awful deflation. My heart gets grazed—pericardium shredded. Blood fills the wrong spaces.

Loop. Restore. Loop. Restore. Loop. Restore.

Each cycle burns like swallowing acid. Each cycle tastes like pennies.

My liver turns to mush under one volley and returns under the next. My spine takes a hit that should steal my legs.

It doesn’t get to.

Loop. Restore.

I stumble.

The momentum should have pinned me. It should have made me a crater ornament. I drop to one knee, mud splashing up my pants, cold water seeping into fabric.

My hands hit the ground and I feel every pebble through the skin, down to the nerve map.

The Ouroboros spins.

Black-gold jitter halos my outline now, flickering at the edges like corrupted footage. Rain hits me and doesn’t behave right. Droplets hesitate. Trails double-expose. The yard can’t keep a clean picture of me.

Bianco makes a strangled sound behind me. He’s trying to run forward. He can’t decide whether he wants to save me or survive me.

I plant my hand on a leg that’s already re-fusing and push up.

My lung reinflates with a wet, viscous suck that makes my ribs ache. Air forces its way back into blood-wet alveoli. I cough and spit a flattened slug that had lodged in my throat. It hits the mud with a soft, stupid plop.

I wipe my mouth with the back of my hand and smear blood across my knuckles like paint.

I keep walking.

It turns into a stroll. A casual, obscene stroll through a storm of depleted-uranium slugs.

Every step is measured. Every step dares them to correct their assumptions.

The cost catches up.

Not as an idea.

As a debt collector.

Hunger clamps down so hard it makes my vision tilt. My tongue sticks to my teeth. Heat rolls through me, then vanishes, leaving a hollow chill in its wake. My hands start to tremble between loops—the tremor that means my body’s negotiating against my will.

Fat burns off first—fast, clean, gone. Then muscle. The loop rebuilds it. The loop eats it again. My body is chewing on tomorrow to stay upright today.

My jaw aches from clenching. My throat tightens like I’m going to vomit, except there’s nothing to give them. My vision grains at the edges with gold-black static. My heart hammers a rhythm that sounds mechanical, wrong-tempo, a misfiring engine refusing to stall.

Somewhere inside my chest a part of me whispers: stop.

I don’t.

I laugh anyway. Blood sprays from my mouth in a bright, wet fan.

“Is that all?” I roar at the drones. “Is that all the ammunition you’ve got? I’m still going! Come on! YOU'RE TRYNA COLLECT MY BOUNTY WITH NOTHING BUT A DOLLAR AND A DREAM. Set phasers from STUN to KILL already! SUMMON UP YOUR DEMONS. HIT ME. FIGHT ME...!!!”

The words shake out of me like a seizure.

I spread my arms wide.

"GIVE ME A HUG!"

Rounds punch through my palms—clean holes, digital-age stigmata—and the flesh closes before the blood can commit to falling.

The pain tries to climb back onto the main thread.

I kick it off.

The drones vibrate. Cooling fans scream at the top end of hearing. Their optics twitch, micro-correcting, hunting for a weakness that refuses to exist in the version of reality I’m running.

Target Hit.
Target Active.
Damage ineffective.
Infinite Loop detected in biological substrate.

Recommendation: Re-evaluate logic.
Recommendation: Escalation. Aim for high-value processing centers.

They adjust.

No more center mass. No more “kill the body.” They aim for the CPU.

The shift is visible in their barrels, a tiny tilt upward. A new solve attempt.

A round grazes my temple and takes an ear and a chunk of scalp with it. Cold rain hits bare skull. For one heartbeat I feel the night air inside my own head, sharp and intimate.

Loop. Restore.

A new ear blooms back into place—pink, wet-looking, perfectly formed—before the old one even finishes falling.

The regrown skin itches. I scratch it once, absent.

Twenty feet.

Ten.

I can see the hesitation in their tracking. The tiny delays. The sensor-lenses flicking as if they’re blinking.

Their code wants a rule that ends me.

It can’t find one.

They were built to kill men.

I step closer, smiling through the blood.

“My turn,” I whisper.

Then I shout, voice splitting from the strain of too many consecutive rewrites. “Firewall? Cute. I brought a rootkit.”

I snap my fingers at the lead drone.

Command: Blue Screen.

No incantation. No request. Just instruction—jammed straight into the logic that tells it what it is.

I feel the command leave me like a thrown knife. Clean. Direct. Mean.

The drone freezes mid-hover. Its red eye goes blank, then washes into a dull, flat blue.

For a fraction of a second the whole machine looks confused, like it’s trying to remember its own name.

It drops.

CRASH.

Metal meets mud. A reminder of the existence of gravity.

Eleven left.

Bianco’s breath catches hard behind me, a sound like he’s swallowing his own heart.

My grin widens until it hurts. The Ouroboros on my shoulder burns hot under my skin.

The air bites like I licked a nine-volt battery.

“Next!” I scream, and the sound glitches in the rain.

Scene 6

We hit the center of the yard. I’m panting, shivering. My body temperature is spiking to 106 degrees. The regeneration is generating too much waste heat. I’m cooking myself from the inside out. Steam is rising from my skin, smelling of copper and burnt hair.

The drones are backing up. They have realized that kinetic weaponry is ineffective against a target that treats causality as a suggestion.

But they aren't retreating. They're grouping.

"Bodhi!" Bianco screams from behind a crate. He’s huddled in the fetal position, his hands over his ears. "They're charging! Look at the chests! They're charging up!"

I look up.

The eleven remaining drones have formed a phalanx. A perfect firing line. Their chest cavities open with a synchronized clack-hiss of hydraulics. Blue light gathers in their cores, a rising whine that sets my teeth on edge.

Plasma.

My smile falters.

Bullets I can loop. Bullets are small. They displace matter, but they don't destroy it. But plasma? Thermal energy concentrated to the point of star-fire? That doesn't leave anything to restore. It vaporizes the hardware. It ionizes the biological substrate. You can't run a System Restore if the hard drive is melted slag.

"Okay," I mutter, wiping a new stream of blood from my nose. "That’s a problem."

I check my mana reserves. Empty. The gauge is flashing red. I spent it all on the light show, on the fear tactics.

I check my stamina. Negative. I’m running on fumes and spite. My glycogen stores are depleted. My muscles are eating themselves.

I look at the drones. The blue light is blinding now, casting long, sharp shadows through the rain. Charge at 90%.

I can't shield this. A standard Cleric Shield would shatter in a microsecond.

I can't heal through this. Even with the Ouroboros, I can't out-regenerate total atomization.

I have to BEAT it.

I look around. The yard is full of shipping containers. Iron. Steel. Carbon. Mountains of mass.

Matter.

My physics textbook from the Academy flashes in my mind. Chapter 4: Equivalent Exchange. Professor Aris explaining the relationship between matter and energy.

E = mc².

Energy equals Mass times the Speed of Light squared.

It’s the most famous equation in history, but nobody really gets it. They think it's about bombs. It’s not about bombs. It’s about Identity.

It means that Mass is just Energy that has slowed down. It means that Energy is just Mass that has decided to go for a run.

Which means... they are the same thing.

If I can just... flip the bit.

I stare at the nearest container. It’s a standard twenty-foot TEU, painted a flaking russet red. Iron oxide. Fe2O3.

I focus my Eye. I don't look at the box. I look into it.

Zoom: x1000. I see the crystal lattice of the steel.
Zoom: x1,000,000. I see the molecular bonds, the electromagnetic forces holding the atoms together.
Zoom: x1,000,000,000.

I see the atoms.

They look like solar systems. A dense nucleus of protons and neutrons, orbited by a cloud of probability where the electrons live.

I find a single atom of Iron-56. Fate. Randomness. It doesn't matter. It’s the one.

Isolate.

The universe falls away. The rain stops. The drones stop. The only thing that exists is me and this atom.

I focus everything I have left—every scrap of mana, every shredded neuron, every drop of willpower—on the sub-atomic particles inside that nucleus.

I don't want to destroy it. I want to invert it.

I see the Protons (Positive). I see the Electrons (Negative).

I assert Admin Privilege.

Command: Invert Polarity.
Target: 1 milligram of Iron.
Action: Spin Reversal.

My brain screams. It feels like I’m trying to lift a mountain with my eyelids. The laws of physics are heavy. They don't want to bend. They have been written this way for fourteen billion years.

But I am not a physicist. I am a Cleric. I am a Heretic. I am the Glitch.

I force the spin to flip.

Property: Matter --> Antimatter.

For a nanosecond, I hold a tiny speck of anti-iron in the middle of a universe of matter.

"This is how the World begins anew, B," I whisper. "Not with a whimper. But with a bang."

I snap my fingers.

"Divinity of Entropy: Song of the Tyrant Sun"

For a fraction of a second, the universe flinches. The light is sucked into the reaction point. Sound is deleted. The rain hovering in the air evaporates into elementary particles.

Then, the annihilation event occurs.

The world doesn't turn white.

It turns Null.

Matter meets Antimatter. They cancel each other out. And in their death, they release 100% of their mass energy.

E = mc².

It’s not an explosion. It’s a correction.

The shockwave moves faster than sound. It moves faster than thought.

I see the shipping container—thirty tons of steel—simply cease to be solid. It unspools. The atoms lose their grip on each other. The binding energy that held them together is released in a catastrophic, joyful shout.

Then the heat hits.

It’s not a wave. It’s a wall. A solid, physical wall of pure thermal expansion.

I have 0.0001 seconds to react.

Execute: Cleric_Shield_v9.0
Error: Insufficient Mana.

Execute: Recusive Regen Loop: Divert_BioMass_to_Epidermis.
Warning: This will result in 90% dermal liquefaction and massive internal trauma.

I grin as my face begins to melt.

Do it.

The blast wave slams into us. I tackle Bianco, driving him into the mud behind the engine block of a rusted forklift. It’s tiny cover, pathetic cover, but it’s mass.

I wrap my body around his, creating a cocoon of flesh and bone. I become the shield.

I feel my back vaporize.

It’s not pain. It’s too fast for pain. It’s just... loss. I feel my skin strip away in layers. The epidermis goes first, turning to carbon dust. Then the dermis. Then the muscle.

I feel my ribs turn to charcoal. I feel the water in my blood flash-boil into steam, inflating my veins until they burst.

The shockwave hits my spine and I feel the vertebrae crack, grinding together like mortar and pestle.

And the noise.
It wasn't a boom. It wasn't a roar.

It was the sound of a god inhaling. It was the sound of reality screaming as a hole was punched through its chest. A high, thin, impossible shriek that shattered glass ten miles away.

Scene 7

Bianco scrambles into the pilot’s seat. His hands are shaking so bad he can barely find the ignition sequence. He keeps missing the biometric reader, his thumb sliding off the glass panel in a smear of oil and sweat.

"Start it up, B!" I yell from the co-pilot’s chair, spinning around in it like a child in a candy store. "Spin the wheel! Pull the lever! Do the thing! The popsicle stand is blow, the candle is lit! LET'SA F***ING GOOOOOOOOOOOOO-"

"I’m TRYING, jackass!" he screams, jamming his thumb onto the reader with enough force to crack the screen. "It's cold! The reactor is cold!"

The engine coughs. It wheezes. It sounds like a smoker with bronchitis trying to run a marathon. The dampeners whine, protesting the sudden demand for power. But then—catch. A roar of combustion. Black smoke billows out the back, instantly swallowed by the storm.

"Yes!" I cheer, punching the dashboard. "She lives! She breathes! She pollutes!"

We lurch into the air just as the yard’s main alarm goes off. A siren wails—high-pitched, frantic, a mechanical scream that cuts through the silence I just created. Spotlights sweep the fog, hunting for the source of the devastation.

"We have company!" Bianco yells, wrestling the yoke as the skimmer fights the crosswinds. "Sensors are lighting up!"

I look at the rear monitor. The visuals are grainy, distorted by the radiation interference, but unnecessary. I can feel them.

Three NAMF interceptors are rising from the garrison loop. Fast. Sleek. They look like sharks made of matte-black hate. Their engines burn with pure blue mana-fire.

"They're locking on!" Bianco cries, his voice jumping an octave. "I can see the radar paint! They have tone!"

I lean back, putting my feet up on the dashboard, crossing my ankles. "Relax. I got this."

"You got this? You're half-dead! You don't have enough mana to light a cigarette!"

"True," I admit. "But I don't need mana. I just need... access."

I close my eyes.

I don't visualize the sky. I don't visualize the interceptors. I visualize the network.

Everything is connected. The radar waves bouncing off our hull are just data packets. The targeting computers in the interceptors are just logic gates processing variables.

Variable: Target_Acquired = True.
Variable: Target_Location = [Latitude, Longitude, Altitude].

I reach out. My mind feels slippery, frictionless. I slide past the encryption keys. I ignore the firewalls. They are looking for magic intrusion; they aren't looking for a syntax error.

I find the data stream connecting the radar dish to the cockpit display.

Edit Property: Opacity.
Edit Property: Radar_Cross_Section.

Set Value: NULL.

I push the command out. It drains me—a sudden, sharp headache that feels like an icepick to the frontal lobe—but the result is instantaneous.

On the monitor, the interceptors veer off. They bank hard left, breaking formation. They’re confused. Their sensors are telling them that the target they were just locking onto has ceased to exist. To them, we are no longer a physical object. We are a ghost. A glitch in the skybox.

"Where did they go?" Bianco asks, banking the skimmer hard to the right, following the coastline. "Why did they turn?"

"They didn't go anywhere," I grin, opening one eye. The gold iris spins lazily, tracking the interceptors as they vanish into the clouds. "We just... adjusted our render distance. To them, we're just a skipped frame."

I laugh, a high, thin sound that barely covers the ringing in my ears. I’m pushing the hardware too hard. I know it. My brain feels like it’s vibrating in a bath of acid. But God, the speed. The absolute control.

For the first time in my life, the world doesn't feel like a cage. It feels like a terminal. And I have the keyboard.

Scene 8

We’re over the Atlantic now. The ocean is a black void beneath us, endless and indifferent. The storm has broken, leaving behind a sky full of cold, distant stars that look down on us with zero empathy.

Bianco has leveled us out at thirty thousand feet, but he’s white-knuckling the yoke like he’s trying to strangle it. The skimmer groans in the jet stream, trembling like a frightened animal.

"This is crazy," he mutters, his eyes fixed on the horizon. "This is absolutely crazy. We just... we just erased a shipping yard. We erased a Sentry Squad."

"It’s aerodynamic!" I correct him. "Usually."

I’m sitting on the floor of the cargo hold now, cross-legged. I’m vibrating. My leg is bouncing up and down at a frequency that is probably annoying, but I can't stop. The energy is trapped under my skin. The Ouroboros on my shoulder is humming, a quiet background process keeping my arm attached to my torso.

"Bodhi," Bianco says, his voice tight. He doesn't look back. "Are you... okay?"

"Okay?" I look at him. "Define okay. Am I functioning? Yes. Am I currently metabolizing adrenaline at a rate that would kill a horse? Also yes. Is my soul technically a piece of malware? Debateable."

"You... you're smiling," he says. The accusation hangs in the air. "We just lost everything. Your father is... gone. The estate is gone. Everything we knew is ash. And you're smiling."

I stop bouncing. The silence stretches, thin and taut, filled only by the drone of the engines.

"I'm not smiling because I'm happy, B," I say, my voice dropping that octave again. The mania bleeds away, leaving something colder, harder. "I'm smiling because if I stop, I have to acknowledge the fact that I'm currently running on a system that has had its emotional core excised with a rusty spoon."

I tap my chest, right over the spot where my heart is beating a frantic, arrhythmic tattoo against my healed ribs.

"I deleted the panic, Bianco. I looked at the fear—the paralyzing, screaming, animal terror of what we just did—and I dragged it to the Recycle Bin. Because if I feel it? If I actually let myself feel the statistical probability of our survival? If I let myself feel the weight of Papa's death?"

I lean forward, the grin returning, sharp and brittle as broken glass.

"I’d crash this ship into the water just to make the noise stop."

Bianco stares at me in the reflection of the cockpit glass. He looks horrified. Good. Horror is a valid output. It means he’s processing the data correctly. He understands the stakes.

"So," I clap my hands, the sound echoing in the empty hold. "Let’s focus on the mission! Rome! Pizza! Sanctuary! I am the CPU, you are the Driver. Keep eyes on the road, and I’ll keep reality from collapsing around us. Deal?"

Bianco swallows. He nods slowly. "Deal."

"Excellent!" I flop back onto the deck plating, staring up at the rivets in the ceiling. "Now, wake me up when we hit the Eurozone. I need to defrag my hard drive. I think I have a memory leak in my amygdala."

Scene 9

I don't sleep. Sleep is for people who need to knit their subconscious back together. Sleep is for people who have dreams. I don't have dreams anymore; I have simulations.

I just stare at the ceiling rivets. I count them. 1,402 rivets. Rivets 45 through 92 show signs of oxidative stress.

I look at the ring on my finger.

Papa’s ring. Obsidian. Cold. It absorbs the ambient light of the cockpit, a tiny black hole on my hand.

I spin it with my thumb. Over and over. A fidget loop.

I think about the Source Code.

The souls—the 1000 nodes I touched in the Shard—they weren't just data. They were files. Complex, encrypted, beautiful files. And if a file can be deleted... it can be restored.

I don't need a resurrection spell. Resurrection is messy. It’s necromancy. It’s guessing. It's trying to paint a portrait from memory.

I need a System Restore.

I need to find the backup.

I hold the ring up to the faint light of the dashboard. The gold in my eyes reflects off the black stone, creating a tiny, inverted universe in the gem's surface.

"I’m going to find the server," I whisper to the ring. "I’m going to find the admin console for this whole stupid, broken reality. And when I do..."

I clench my fist, feeling the metal bite into my skin.

"...I’m going to fix the bug."

It’s not a prayer. Prayers are requests. This is a notification. A patch note.

I close my eyes and let the hum of the engine vibrate through my bones. I am a weapon now. I am a tool. And tools have a purpose.

Object: World.
Status: Broken.
Action: Repair.

Scene 10

Dawn breaks over the Mediterranean. The light is sickly, filtered through the smog of the Industrial Zones, turning the clouds a bruised purple.

But then—

Rome.

It hangs in the sky like a chandelier made of gothic stone and neon.

The Holy Roman Empire. The Eternal City. Floating a kilometer above the earth, tethered by crackling ley-lines that hum with raw mana. It is a jewel of impossible architecture, spires of white marble piercing the smog layer, bathed in perpetual, artificial sunlight.

I stand up and press my face to the cockpit glass, leaving a greasy smear.

"Whoa," Bianco breathes. "The Golden Cage."

I look at it. My Ajna flares, overlaying the view with data.

I don't see the beauty. I see the cost.

I see the flow of power—thick, pulsing arteries of mana being sucked up from the earth below. I see the structural stress points on the suspension cables. I see the defensive wards shimmering like heat haze, a complex lattice of 'Keep Out' signals.

And I see the shadow it casts. The miles of slums underneath, living in the permanent eclipse of the city above.

It’s massive. It’s arrogant. It’s a city built on the backs of the 25 million souls they harvested to fuel this "Paradise." It is a monument to efficient predation.

But I don't feel awe. I feel... hunger.

"It’s not a cage, B," I say, and my voice is steady. The mania has cooled into a cold, hard focus. "It’s a target."

I trace the outline of the Vatican on the glass. The Great Dome. The seat of the High Council.

"That’s where the server is," I say. "The Vault. The Philosopher's Stone. The Root Directory."

I look at Bianco. The fear is still there in his eyes, but there’s something else now. Hope? Or just the desperate realization that he’s strapped into a rollercoaster that has no brakes.

When I take over the world, this will be the first place I come back to.

"Take us in," I command.

Scene 11

We descend toward the Ludus Magnus boundary. The traffic is thick—luxury skimmers, cargo haulers, military patrols. We slip into the stream, just another piece of debris in the river of commerce.

I watch the advertisements flashing on the holographic billboards that line the approach vector.

MANA-MAX SUPPLEMENTS! Guaranteed awakening or your credits back!
INDULGENCE CREDITS: Buy Now, Sin Later! Pre-approved forgiveness for Tier 1 Citizens!
Sentry Drones: Keeping You Safe. Keeping You Pure.

It’s a system. A marketplace. A corrupt, bloated operating system that thinks it’s invincible because no one has ever tried to access the kernel.

I flex my fingers. Black sparks dance between my knuckles. The Ouroboros tattoo on my shoulder burns with a cold, comforting heat.

"They think they’re safe," I murmur. "They think they wrote the code."

I turn to Bianco. The gold in my eyes is pulsing a steady, rhythmic beat. A countdown.

"When we land, we don't just survive," I say. "We log in. We learn their syntax. We find their exploits."

I grin. It’s not the manic, terrifying grin of the crater. It’s the sharp, predatory smile of a hacker who just found the password taped to the monitor.

"And then... we crash the server."

Chapter 13 - END
